Nature of Badal al-Kitabah as Both Price and Sale and Distinction from Salam
Explaining the dual nature of kitabah consideration and distinction from salam contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the dual nature of badal al-kitabah and apply the Hanafi ruling that power to pay is not a condition for validity of kitabah.

Core Idea
Badal al-kitabah is both price and sold item; its dual nature determines annulment and power condition.

This passage addresses a critical question in Hanafi fiqh: what happens when a mukatib (slave under kitabah) cannot pay? The answer reveals a unique dual nature of the consideration (badal al-kitabah) that influences both the annulment of the contract and the conditions for its validity. By the end, you will understand how the Hanafi school uses this duality to rule that lack of power to pay does not invalidate the contract at inception, and how it contrasts with the salam contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The passage opens with a question: when a mukatib becomes unable to pay, does that annul the kitabah? Recognize that this is a question about the effect of a condition on the contract's validity.
The answer hinges on the nature of badal al-kitabah.
2
Understand the dual nature of badal al-kitabah
The author explains that badal al-kitabah has two aspects: from one perspective it is a price (thaman), from another it is a sold item (mabee'). This dual nature allows the Hanafis to apply different rulings to annulment and conditions.
As a sale, inability to pay causes annulment; as a price, power to pay is a condition.
3
Locate the proof (dalil)
The author cites ijma (consensus) as proof that one can purchase from a person who does not own the price. This establishes that power over the price is not a condition for contract validity.
The exact phrase: 'للاجماع على جواز ابتياع من لا يملك الثمن'.
4
Contrast with salam contract
The author distinguishes kitabah from salam. In salam, the muslam fihi is always the subject matter (thaman) in every respect, so power over it is a condition. In kitabah, the dual nature allows leniency.
The basis of kitabah is ease; of salam is strictness.
5
Apply the ruling
Synthesize: A kitabah contract is valid even if the slave has no power to pay initially. If the slave later becomes unable to pay, the contract may be annulled from the sale aspect.
This is the mufta bihi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سوال: مکاتب کا ادائیگی سے عاجز ہو جانا موجب فسخ ہوتا ہے اور یہ مبیع ہونے کی علامت ہے۔ جواب: بدل کتابت میں دو جہتیں ہیں من وجہ ثمن ہے اور من وجہ بیع۔ پس ہم نے دونوں جہتوں پر عمل کرتے ہوئے کہا ہے کہ مبیع ہونے کے لحاظ سے کتابت فسخ ہو جائے گی اور ثمن ہونے کے لحاظ سے ادائیگی پر قدرت کا ہونا شرط ہوگا۔ بخلاف سلم کے کہ اس میں مسلم فیہ معقود علیہ ہوتا ہے۔ یعنی ہر لحاظ سے ثمن ہوتا ہے یہاں تک کہ قبضہ سے پہلے اس کا استبدال جائز نہیں پس مسلم فیہ پر قدرت کا ہونا شرط ہوگا جیسے بیع میں ہوتا ہے کہ اسکے جائز ہونے کے لئے بائع کا مبیع پر قادر ہونا شرط نہیں ہے (للاجماع على جواز ابتياع من لا يملك الثمن) اسی طرح کتابت میں بھی یہ شرط نہ ہوگا کہ غلام کو مال پر قدرت حاصل ہو۔

قوله ولان مبنى الكتابة ... الخ: کتابت اور سلم میں ایک وجہ فرق یہ بھی ہے کہ کتابت کا مدار آسانی اور ڈھیل پر ہوتا ہے۔ یعنی چشم پوشی کا قصد پہلے ہی سے ہوتا ہے تا کہ غلام آزاد ہو جائے۔ پس اگر فی الحال ادائیگی کی قرار داد ہو تب بھی ظاہر یہی ہے کہ آقا اسے مہلت دے گا بخلاف بیع سلم کے کہ اس کا مدار تنگی پر ہے کہ جانبین میں سے ہر ایک اپنا حق کسب کر لینا چاہتا ہے۔ پس جس وقت واجب ہو اسی وقت وصول کرے گا۔
Question: The mukatib's inability to pay causes annulment and it is a sign of being a sold item (mabee'). Answer: The consideration of kitabah (badal al-kitabah) has two aspects: in one aspect it is a price (thaman) and in another aspect it is a sale (bay'). So we, acting upon both aspects, have said: with respect to being a sale, kitabah will be annulled; and with respect to being a price, the condition of having power to pay is required. Contrary to salam, because in it, the muslam fihi is the subject matter of the contract (ma'qud alayh). That is, it is a price (thaman) in every respect, to the extent that its substitution before possession is not permissible. So power over the muslam fihi is a condition, just as in a sale where it is not a condition for its validity that the seller have power over the sold item (mabee') due to the consensus (ijma) on the permissibility of purchasing from one who does not own the price. Similarly, in kitabah, this condition will not apply that the slave has power over wealth. His statement (qalahu): 'And because the basis of kitabah...' – Another point of difference between kitabah and salam is that the basis of kitabah is ease and leniency. That is, the intention of turning a blind eye is there from the beginning so that the slave becomes free. So even if payment is arranged immediately, it appears that the master will give him respite. Contrary to the sale of salam, whose basis is strictness, because each of the two parties wants to obtain their right. So when it becomes due, he will receive it at that time.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
Passage type: Ruling with proof. This passage addresses a key question: what happens when a mukatab (slave under kitabah contract) becomes unable to pay the installments? The answer demonstrates the unique dual nature of badal al-kitabah (the consideration in kitabah). It is both a thaman (price) and a mabee' (sold item) depending on perspective. From the perspective of being a sale, inability to pay annuls the contract; from the perspective of being a price, power to pay is a condition. The author contrasts this with salam (a forward sale of fungible goods), where the muslam fihi is always the subject matter (ma'qud alayh) and thus power over it is a condition. The author supports the Hanafi position by referencing the consensus (ijma) that one can purchase from a person who does not own the price, analogizing kitabah to that. Then, a second distinction is added: the basis of kitabah is ease and leniency – the master intends to give the slave a chance to earn freedom, so he will give him time, unlike salam where strictness prevails.

Key Terms
بدل کتابت (badal al-kitabah): The consideration (payment) that the slave agrees to pay in return for his freedom under a kitabah contract.
مبیع (mabee'): The item sold; here it refers to the status of the kitabah contract as being analogous to a sale when considering annulment.
سلم (salam): A forward sale contract where the price is paid immediately and the goods are delivered later; used here as a contrasting contract.
اجماع (ijma): Scholarly consensus taken as a source of law; here it supports the permissibility of buying from one who does not own the price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the dual nature of badal al-kitabah according to the Hanafi school?
A) It is only a price (thaman).
B) It is only a sale (mabee').
C) It is both a price and a sale depending on perspective.
D) It is neither a price nor a sale.

Key Takeaway
Badal al-kitabah is both a price and a sold item; thus, power to pay is not a condition for contract validity, but inability may cause annulment.

Validity of Kitabah with Installment Clauses: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Analyzing the validity of kitabah when the master mentions installments explicitly or implicitly

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two narrations regarding whether a master's statement conditioning freedom on payment in installments without explicit kitabah wording constitutes a valid kitabah, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Kitabah requires explicit wording; mere mention of installments does not create kitabah per the stronger narration.

The Hanafi school has a nuanced view on what constitutes a valid kitabah contract. When a master explicitly uses kitabah language and adds installment terms, the contract is valid by istihsan. But when the master merely conditions freedom on payment in installments without any kitabah-specific wording, a dispute arises between two companions of Imam Muhammad. Understanding this dispute shows how subtle differences in wording can have major legal consequences for the slave's status.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two scenarios: (1) master explicitly says 'I have placed upon you a debt payable in installments; if you pay, you are free' – this is valid kitabah by istihsan. (2) Master says 'If you pay me a thousand dirhams in monthly installments of one hundred, you are free' – here there is a dispute.
2
Analyze the reason for istihsan in the first scenario
By qiyas, the installment clause would be an extraneous addition (za'id), making the contract invalid. But istihsan overrides qiyas because the meaning of kitabah (exchange of payment for freedom) is present. The installment is merely a mode of payment, not a contradiction.
Istihsan is a core Hanafi principle that gives weight to the underlying objective over strict analogy.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute in the second scenario
Two narrations from companions of Imam Muhammad: Abu Sulayman says the statement is kitabah because installments indicate an obligation (wujub) that can only come from kitabah. Abu Hafs says it is not kitabah because no kitabah-specific wording is present; installments are generic.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school, not a disagreement with outside schools.
4
Determine the tarjih and its basis
Fakhral-Islam al-Marghinani in al-Mabsut declares Abu Hafs's view as more correct (asahh). The evidence: if the master had said 'If you pay me a thousand this month, you are free,' that would clearly not be kitabah; so analogically, the same applies here. Installment is not a characteristic of kitabah.
The tarjih relies on the absence of any distinctive kitabah feature in the wording.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
If it is kitabah (Abu Sulayman's view), the slave becomes a mukatab with protections: cannot be sold, can earn, etc. If it is not kitabah (mufta bihi), the slave remains a regular slave and can be sold. The master must still honor the conditional promise, but the slave does not have mukatab status.
This difference has significant implications for the slave's legal capacity and treatment.

پھر کافی میں ہے کہ یہ صحت منی بر استحسان ہے قیاس کی رو سے صحیح نہیں اس لئے کہ ہمارے نزدیک کتابت میں نجوم اور قسطوں کو ذکر کرنا ایک زائد بات ہے جس کا وجود دو عدم برابر ہے۔ پس قول مولی قد جعلت علیک الف درهم باقی رہا جو بظاہر تمل ضربیہ بھی ہے اور فاذا اديتها فانت حر تعلیق ہے۔ وجہ استحسان یہ ہے کہ عقد میں اعتبار معانی کا ہوتا ہے اور یہاں کتابت کے معنی موجود ہیں اور جب کتابت بصورت اطلاق صحیح ہو جاتی ہے تو تفسیر کی صورت میں بطریق اولیٰ صحیح ہوگی۔

قوله ولو قال اذا ادیت .... الخ: اور اگر آقا نے اپنے غلام سے یہ کہا کہ اگر تو نے ماہانہ سو درہم کر کے مجھے ہزار درہم ادا کر دیے تو تو آزاد ہے تو شیخ ابوسلیمان موسیٰ بن سلیمان جو ز جانی (صاحب امام محمد) کی روایت میں آقا کا قول مذکور مکاتبہ ہے۔ اس واسطے کہ قسط کرنا اس امر کی دلیل ہے کہ آقا نے اس کو غلام کے ذمہ واجب کیا ہے اور یہ وجوب بذریعہ کتابت ہی ہوگا۔ کیونکہ تنجیم کا استعمال تخفیف و تیسیر کے لئے ہوتا ہے اور تخفیف مال میں ہوتی ہے اور مال کا وجوب کتابت سے ہوتا ہے (لان المولى لا يستوجب على عبده دینا الا بالكتابه) پس ہم نے جان لیا کہ اس تنجیم سے آقا کا مقصد ایجاب بدل ہے۔

قوله وفى نسخة أبي حفص .... الخ: اور شیخ ابو حفص کبیر احمد بن حفص متوفی ۲۱۷ھ (صاحب امام محمد) کے نسخہ (روایت) میں قول مذکور مکاتبہ نہ ہوگا۔ فخر الاسلام نے مبسوط میں کہا ہے کہ یہی اصح ہے۔ بدلیل آنکہ اگر آقا یہ کہے اذا اديت الي الفا في هذا الشهر فانت حر تو یہ کتابت نہیں ہوتی پس ایسے ہی قول مذکور میں بھی نہ ہوگی۔ کیونکہ یہاں کوئی ایسا لفظ نہیں پایا گیا جو کتابت کے خصائص میں سے ہوا۔ اس لئے کہ کل شهر مائة سے تنجیم نکلی اور تنجیم خصائص کتابت میں سے نہیں ہے۔ یہاں تک کہ اس کو کتابت کی تفسیر قرار دیا جائے اس واسطے کہ تنجیم کبھی تو ہر قسم کے دین میں ہو سکتی ہے اور کبھی خود کتابت میں بھی نہیں ہوتی۔
His statement: 'And whoever says to his slave...' – In al-Jami' al-Saghir: A person said to his slave: 'I have placed upon you a thousand dirhams which you will pay in installments. The first installment is such, and the last installment is such – i.e., he specified both amount and time – then he said: If you pay these, you are free; if you become incapable, you remain a slave.' Then this is a valid kitabah because the master explained the kitabah with interpretation. Now, these rulings pertain to this slave: his sale is no longer permissible; it is permissible to annul the kitabah by mutual consent; if the slave has paid some of the consideration, it is no longer a place to be given for expiation (kaffarah), contrary to a slave whose freedom is suspended upon payment of wealth, because for him these rulings are opposite. Then, in al-Kafi, it is said that this validity is based on istihsan (juristic preference), and it is not valid by qiyas (analogy), because according to us, mentioning installments and installments in kitabah is an additional matter whose existence is like non-existence. So the master's statement 'I have placed upon you a thousand dirhams' remains, which is a statement of ownership (?), and 'if you pay them, you are free' is a suspension. The reason for istihsan is that in contracts, consideration is given to meanings, and here the meaning of kitabah is present; and when kitabah is valid in the absolute form, then in the form of interpretation it is valid a fortiori (bi tariq al-awla).

His statement: 'And if he said: If you pay...' – And if the master said to his slave: 'If you pay me a thousand dirhams in monthly installments of one hundred each, you are free,' then according to the narration of Shaykh Abu Sulayman Musa ibn Sulayman al-Juzjani (companion of Imam Muhammad), this statement is kitabah. Because installing indicates that the master has made it obligatory upon the slave, and this obligation is only through kitabah. Since installment is used for ease and facilitation, and ease is in wealth, and the obligation of wealth is from kitabah (because the master does not impose a debt on his slave except through kitabah). So we know that by this installment, the master intends to impose the consideration.

His statement: 'And in the narration of Abu Hafs...' – And according to Shaykh Abu Hafs al-Kabir Ahmad ibn Hafs (d. 217 AH, companion of Imam Muhammad), in his narration, the mentioned statement is not kitabah. Fakhral-Islam said in al-Mabsut that this is more correct (asahh). The evidence is: if the master says 'If you pay me a thousand in this month, you are free,' that is not kitabah, so likewise here. Because no word is found that is from the characteristics of kitabah. Since 'every month one hundred' produces installment, and installment is not from the characteristics of kitabah, to the extent that it can be considered an interpretation of kitabah, because installment sometimes occurs in every type of debt, and sometimes it is not (found) in kitabah itself.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
Passage type: Intra-Hanafi dispute with istihsan reasoning. This section examines whether a master's statement conditioning freedom on payment of a specified sum in installments constitutes a valid kitabah contract. Two scenarios are presented:

First scenario: The master explicitly says 'I have placed upon you a thousand dirhams, payable in installments of X and Y; if you pay, you are free; if you fail, you are a slave.' This is unanimously considered valid kitabah, established by istihsan. The author cites al-Kafi: by qiyas, this would not be valid because the installment clause is an addition that has no legal effect (like non-existence), but istihsan gives weight to the underlying meaning of kitabah (master's intent to free the slave in exchange for payment). Since absolute kitabah (without installments) is valid, the interpretative form is even more clearly valid.

Second scenario: The master says 'If you pay me a thousand dirhams in monthly payments of one hundred, you are free.' Here there is a dispute between two narrations from the companions of Imam Muhammad. Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani holds that this is kitabah because the mention of installments indicates an obligation (wujub) that can only come from kitabah (since a master cannot impose a debt on his slave except by kitabah). The installment points to the master's intent to make the slave's freedom conditional on payment, which is the essence of kitabah.

Abu Hafs al-Kabir, however, holds that this is not kitabah. His reasoning: there is no explicit wording that is specific to kitabah; the installment method is not a distinctive feature of kitabah; it can apply to any debt. Furthermore, if the master had said 'If you pay a thousand this month, you are free', that would clearly not be kitabah; so likewise here. Fakhral-Islam al-Marghinani (in al-Mabsut) declares Abu Hafs's view as more correct (asahh). This disagreement shows the subtlety of distinguishing a true kitabah from a mere conditional promise of freedom.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference: a method of reasoning that overrides qiyas for a stronger reason; here it validates kitabah with installments.
تنجيم (tanjim): The specification of installments (stars/portions) in a debt; here it refers to the payment schedule.
تعليق (ta'liq): Suspension of a legal consequence upon a condition; here freedom is suspended upon payment.
نسخة (nuskhah): Narration or version of a transmission; here it refers to the narration reported by Abu Hafs as opposed to Abu Sulayman.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the relied-upon (asahh) position regarding a master saying 'If you pay me a thousand dirhams in monthly installments of one hundred, you are free'?
A) It is a valid kitabah according to all narrations.
B) It is not a valid kitabah according to Abu Hafs, and this is considered more correct.
C) It is valid only if the master says 'kitabah' explicitly.
D) It is invalid because installments are not allowed.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that a master's statement conditioning freedom on payment in installments does not constitute kitabah unless it includes explicit kitabah wording; the mere mention of installments is insufficient.

Ruling When a Mukatab Pays with Wine
Ruling on payment of prohibited substances in kitabah contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling when a mukatab pays with a prohibited substance (wine) and identify the qiyas (analogy) used to derive the compensation rule.

Core Idea
Paying with wine in a kitabah contract frees the slave but requires him to pay the full price because the contract is fasid, analogous to a corrupt sale.

In this passage, al-Hidayah presents a key ruling on a mukatab who pays with wine (or blood) as consideration. The Hanafi school holds that such payment does not invalidate the manumission, but the contract becomes corrupt (fasid), requiring the slave to earn and pay his full market price (qimah). The ruling is derived by analogy to a corrupt sale (bay' fasid) where the sold item is destroyed. We will unpack this analogy, the rules on price adjustment, and a contrasting case with cloth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a mukatab paying wine (a prohibited, non-mal substance) as the kitabah consideration. The Hanafi ruling: the contract is fasid (corrupt), but the slave becomes free upon payment. He must compensate the master by working for his price (qimah).
Important: manumission is valid, but the contract is not sound.
2
Locate the dalil – the qiyas
The author uses analogy: 'just as in a corrupt sale when the sold item is destroyed'. The asl (original case) is a corrupt sale where the buyer destroys the item; the far' (new case) is the kitabah contract with wine payment. The illa (effective cause) is that in both, the substitute (item or slave's freedom) is irretrievable, so the price is wajib.
The qiyas is jaliy (clear).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
In the asl, when the sold item is destroyed, the buyer must pay its value (qimah) because returning the item is impossible. Similarly, in the far', the slave's person (raqaba) was to be returned but becomes impossible due to manumission, so he must pay his price.
The price is the slave's market value at the time of the contract.
4
Note the sub-rules on price adjustment
The price cannot be less than the named amount (the master is not satisfied with less), but it may exceed it because the slave implicitly agreed to any increase to gain freedom. This mirrors the rule in a corrupt sale where the price is whatever the value turns out to be.
Zahir al-riwayah supports this.
5
Distinguish the cloth kitabah case
The passage contrasts with a kitabah on a specific cloth: paying a cloth does not free the slave because the master's intent is uncertain due to the variety of cloth types. In the price case, uncertainty only makes the contract fasid, not void, and the price remains determinable.
This distinction highlights the difference between fasid and batil.

Source Text

عین خمر ادا کرنے سے آزاد ہو گیا تو اپنی قیمت میں مزدوری کرے
واذا عتق باداء عين الخمر لزمه ان يسعى فى قيمته لانه وجب عليه رد رقبته لفساد العقد وقد تعذر بالعتق فيجب رد قيمته كما فى البيع الفاسد اذا تلف المبيع . قال ولا ينقص عن المسمى ويزاد عليه لانه عقد فاسد فيجب القيمة عند هلاك المبدل بالغة ما بلغت كما فى البيع الفاسد وهذا لان المولى ما رضى بالنقصان والعبد رضى بالزيادة كيلا يبطل حقه فى العتق اصلا فتجب القيمة بالغة ما بلغت وفيما اذا کاتبه على قيمته يعتق باداء القيمة لانه هو البدل وامكن اعتبار معنى العقد فيه واثر الجهالة في الفساد بخلاف ما اذا كاتبه على ثوب حيث لا يعتق باداء ثوب لانه لا يوقف فيه على مراد العاقد لاختلاف اجناس الثوب فلا يثبت العتق بدون ارادته
ترجمہ جب وہ آزاد ہو گیا عین خمر ادا کر کے تو لازم ہے اس پر یہ کہ معایت کرے اپنی قیمت میں ۔ کیونکہ اس پر اپنا رقبہ پھیرنا واجب ہوا فساد عقد کی وجہ سے حالانکہ یہ عشق کیوجہ سے متعذر ہو گیا تو اپنی قیمت واپس کرنا واجب ہوگا جیسے بیج فاسد میں ہوتا ہے جب صبیح تلف ہو جائے اور کم نہیں کی جائے گی بیان کردہ مقدار سے اور اس پر زیادتی کی جاسکتی ہے۔ کیونکہ یہ تو عقد فاسد ہے تو مبدل تلف ہونے پر قیمت واجب ہوگی جہاں تک بھی پہنچے جیسے بیچ فاسد میں ہوتا ہے۔ اور یہ اس لئے ہے کہ آقا نقصان پر راضی نہیں ہوا اور غلام زیادتی پر راضی ہو گیا تاکہ محق میں اس کا حق باطل نہ ہو۔ لہذا قیمت واجب ہوگی جہاں تک بھی پہنچے ۔ اور غلام کو اس کی قیمت پر مکاتب کرنے کی صورت میں اداء قیمت سے آزاد اس لئے ہو جائے گا کہ قیمت ہی عوض ہے اور اس میں معنی عقد کا لقبار کرنا ممکن ہے اور جہالت کا اثر صرف فساد میں ہے بخلاف اس کے جب کپڑے پر مکاتب کیا کہ کپڑا ادا کرنے سے آزاد نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ اس میں عاقد کی مراد پر آگہی نہیں ہو سکتی۔ اجناس ثوب کے اختلاف کی وجہ سے پس اس کے ارادہ کے بغیر آزادی ثابت نہ ہوگی۔
تشریح قوله واذا عتق ... الخ ۔ مسئلہ مذکورہ میں جب مکاتب بعید شراب ادا کر کے آزاد ہو گیا تو وہ اپنی قیمت میں سعایت کریگا۔ یعنی اپنی قیمت کما کر آقا کو دے گا۔ کیونکہ یہاں فاسد عقد کی وجہ سے رد رقبہ واجب ہے مگر اسکے آزاد ہو جانے کی وجہ سے رہ رقبہ متعذر ہے۔ اس لئے قیمت واجب ہوگی۔ جیسے بیج فاسد میں اگر مشتری کے پاس طبع ہلاک ہو جائے تو قیمت واجب ہوتی ہے لیکن ظاہر الروایہ وہی ہے جو پہلے مذکور ہوا۔ قوله ولا ينقص الخ ۔ جس قیمت میں مکاتب مذکور سعایت کرے گا اس میں اس مقدار سے کم نہیں کیا جائے گا جو آقا کی طرف بیان ہوئی تھی۔ ہاں اس میں اضافہ ہو سکتا ہے جہاں تک بھی ہو۔ اس لئے کہ یہ عقد فاسد واقع ہوا تھا جیسے بیچ فاسد میں مبیع تلغب ہونے کے وقت اس کی قیمت واجب ہوتی ہے جتنی بھی ہو ایسے ہی یہاں بھی واجب ہوگی۔
قوله وهذا لان المولى ... الخ - او پر جو یہ مذکور ہوا کہ قیمت مسمی سے کم نہیں کی جائے گی ہاں زائد ہوسکتی ہے اس کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ آقا نے عقد کتابت کو مقدار سمی میں واجب کیا ہے تو وہ اس سے کم پر راضی نہیں ہوا۔ البتہ غلام زائد مقدار سے راضی ہو گیا ہے تا کہ اس کا حق عشق فوت نہ ہو جائے اس لئے کہ اگر وہ زائد مقدار سے راضی نہیں ہوتا تو آقا اس کو آزاد کرنے سے باز رہے گا اور مکاتب کے ہاتھ سے شرف حریت جاتا رہے گا۔ نیز اس لئے بھی کہ جب مکاتب نے عقد فاسد پر اقدام کیا ہے۔ جب کہ وہ جانتا ہے کہ عقد فاسد میں قیمت واجب ہوتی ہے تو وہ قیمت کی ادائیگی سے راضی ہو گیا۔ گو قیمت مقدار سمی سے زائد ہو۔
قوله وفيما كاتب ... الخ - مسئلہ کے شروع میں علی خمر او خنزیر کے بعد جو او علی قیمتہ کہا تھا یہ اسی کی طرف راجع ہے۔ مطلب یہ ہے کہ اگر غلام کو اسکی قیمت پر مکاتب کیا تو وہ اپنی قیمت ادا کر کے آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ قیمت ہی اس کا عوض ہے اور اس میں معنی عقد کتابت کا اعتبار کرنا بھی ممکن ہے۔ بایں طور کہ مکاتب اتنی قیمت ادا کرے جس میں قیمت لگانے والوں کو کوئی اختلاف نہ رہے۔ مثلاً ایک نے اس کی قیمت تمیں درہم لگائی دوسرے نے ۳۵ تیسرے نے ۴۰ اور مکاتب نے ۴۰ ادا کر دیئے تو وہ اپنی قیمت ادا کرنے والا ہو گیا۔ قوله واثر الجهالة ... الخ - سوال کا جواب ہے۔ سوال یہ ہے کہ کپڑے کی طرح یہاں قیمت بھی تو مجہول ہے تو جیسے کپڑے پر مکاتب کرنے کی صورت میں کپڑا ادا کرنے سے آزادی نہیں ہوتی۔ ایسے ہی قیمت کی ادائیگی سے بھی آزادی نہیں ہونی چاہیے بلکہ عقد باطل ہو جانا چاہیے؟
جواب کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ قیمت کی جہالت کا اثر صرف عقد کے فاسد ہونے میں ہے کہ اس سے عقد فاسد ہوگا نہ کہ باطل اور فاسد عقد میں یہی قیمت واجب ہوتی ہے۔ بخلاف کپڑے کے کہ خالی کپڑا کہنے سے آقا کی مراد معلوم نہیں ہوسکتی۔ کیونکہ کپڑے کی اجناس
مختلف ہیں ۔
When he becomes free by paying the specific wine, it is necessary for him to strive for his price, because it became obligatory upon him to return his person due to the corruption of the contract, and that became impossible due to manumission, so the return of his price is obligatory, just as in a corrupt sale when the sold item is destroyed. He said: It is not reduced from the named amount and is increased upon it, because it is a corrupt contract, so the price is obligatory upon destruction of the substitute, whatever it reaches, just as in a corrupt sale. And this is because the master did not agree to the reduction, and the slave agreed to the increase so that his right to manumission is not nullified at all, so the price is obligatory whatever it reaches. And in the case where he made him a mukatab for his price, he becomes free by paying the price, because it is the substitute and it is possible to consider the meaning of the contract in it, and the effect of ignorance is only in the corruption, unlike if he made him a mukatab for a cloth, where he does not become free by paying a cloth, because the intention of the contracting party cannot be known due to the different types of cloth, so manumission is not established without his intention.
Translation: When he became free by paying the specific wine, it is obligatory upon him to strive for his price. Because it became obligatory to return his person due to the corruption of the contract, and it became impossible due to manumission, so returning his price is obligatory, like in a corrupt sale when the sold item is destroyed. It will not be reduced from the stated amount and can be increased, because it is a corrupt contract, so the price is obligatory when the substitute is destroyed, whatever it reaches, like in a corrupt sale. And this is because the master did not consent to the reduction, and the slave consented to the increase so that his right to freedom is not invalidated. Therefore, the price is obligatory whatever it reaches. And when the slave is made a mukatab for his price, he becomes free by paying the price because the price is the substitute and it is possible to consider the meaning of the contract in it, and the effect of ignorance is only in corruption, unlike when he is made a mukatab for a cloth, where he does not become free by paying a cloth, because the intention of the contracting party cannot be known due to the different types of cloth. Thus, freedom is not established without his intention.
Explanation of his statement "and when he becomes free...": In the mentioned issue, when the mukatab becomes free by paying wine, he will strive for his price, i.e., earn his price and give it to the master. Because here due to the corrupt contract, returning the person is obligatory, but due to his becoming free, returning the person is impossible, so the price is obligatory, like in a corrupt sale if the sold item perishes with the buyer, the price is obligatory. But the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah) is what was mentioned earlier. His statement "and it is not reduced": In the price for which the mentioned mukatab will strive, it will not be reduced from the amount stated by the master, but it can be increased as much as possible. Because this was a corrupt contract, like in a corrupt sale when the sold item perishes, its price is obligatory whatever it is. Similarly here.
His statement "and this is because the master...": The reason for what was mentioned that the price will not be reduced from the named amount but can be increased is that the master made the kitabah contract obligatory at the named amount, so he is not satisfied with less. However, the slave has agreed to the increased amount so that his right to freedom is not lost, because if he does not agree to the increase, the master will refrain from freeing him and the slave will lose the honor of freedom. Also, because when the slave undertook a corrupt contract while knowing that in a corrupt contract the price is obligatory, he is satisfied with paying the price even if it exceeds the named amount.
His statement "and in what he made him a mukatab...": This refers to what was said earlier after "on wine or pork" when he said "on his price". The meaning is that if the slave is made a mukatab for his price, he becomes free by paying his price, because the price is his substitute and it is possible to consider the meaning of the kitabah contract in it. For example, if one appraiser values him at 30 dirhams, another at 35, a third at 40, and the mukatab pays 40, then he has paid his price. His statement "and the effect of ignorance...": This is the answer to a question. The question is: Just like a cloth, the price is also unknown, so why does paying the price result in freedom while paying a cloth does not? Shouldn't the contract be void? The answer is that the effect of ignorance of the price is only that the contract becomes corrupt (fasid), not void (batil), and in a corrupt contract this price is obligatory. Unlike a cloth, because saying "a cloth" does not reveal the master's intention due to the different types of cloth.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from qiyas (analogy) concerning a mukatab who pays with a prohibited substance like wine. The central ruling is that the mukatab becomes free upon payment, but the contract is corrupt (fasid) because wine is not property. Therefore, the slave must compensate the master by working for his price (qimah). The price cannot be less than the named amount but may be more, because the master only agreed to the named minimum, and the slave agreed to any increase to secure his freedom. This is analogous to a corrupt sale (bay' fasid) where the sold item is destroyed: the buyer must pay the price. The passage also discusses a sub-rule: if a slave is made a mukatab for his own price (which is unknown), he becomes free by paying it, because the price is the substitute and ignorance only makes the contract corrupt (fasid) rather than void (batil). This is contrasted with a kitabah on a specific cloth, where payment of a cloth does not bring freedom because the master's intent is unclear due to the variety of cloth types. The commentary emphasizes that the relied-upon position (zahir al-riwayah) is as stated, and the slave must pay the full price even above the named amount.

Key Terms
مکاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a kitabah contract with his master to earn his freedom by paying specified sums.
عین خمر (ayn khamr): Specific wine (a prohibited substance) offered as payment in a kitabah contract.
قیمت (qimah): The market price of the slave, which he must pay as compensation when the substitute (wine) is invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a mukatab pays with wine, what is the status of the kitabah contract and the slave?
A) The contract is void (batil); the slave remains enslaved.
B) The contract is corrupt (fasid); the slave becomes free but must pay his price.
C) The contract is valid; the slave becomes free and owes nothing.
D) The contract is suspended until the wine is replaced.

Key Takeaway
A mukatab who pays with a prohibited substance like wine becomes free, but the contract is fasid and he must pay his full market price, which may exceed the named amount.

Kitabah on a Specific Item Belonging to Another
Ruling on validity of kitabah when payment is a specific item owned by another

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on kitabah when the payment is a specific item owned by another, distinguish the exception for dirhams, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Core Idea
A kitabah contract on a specific item owned by another is invalid; dirhams are an exception because they become a debt.

In this passage from Al-Hidayah, we examine the validity of a kitabah contract when the agreed payment is a specific item (ayn) owned by another. The author presents the general Hanafi ruling, an important exception for dirhams, and an intra-madhhab dispute. Understanding this ruling requires appreciating the condition of ability to deliver in commutative contracts and how it differs from marriage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage begins by stating that making a mukatab slave pay with a specific item belonging to another is not permissible. This is the general ruling.
The act is kitabah with a specific item (ayn).
2
Note the exception for dirhams
If the payment is specified as dirhams that belong to another, the contract is valid because dirhams are not considered specified in commutative contracts; they become a debt in the slave's dhimmah.
This exception is based on the nature of dirhams as fungible items.
3
Understand the intra-Hanafi dispute
A narration from Abu Hanifa via Hasan ibn Ziyad permits the contract for any specific item, with the condition that if the slave later obtains and delivers it, he is freed; if unable, he returns to slavery.
The opposing view compares the ability to deliver to the dower in marriage.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The major response is that in commutative contracts like sale, ability to deliver at the time of contract is a condition because the contract is susceptible to rescission. Marriage is different because the dower is subsidiary.
The author uses qiyas to sale and distinguishes marriage.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The majority view (Zahir al-Riwayah) is relied upon: the contract is invalid for a specific item belonging to another. Dirhams are the only exception.
The permissive narration is considered weak for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال و کذالک ان کاتبه على شيء بعينه لغيره لم يجز لانه لا يقدر على تسليمه ومراده شيء يتعين بالتعين حتى لو قال كاتبتك على هذه الالف الدرهم وهى لغيره جاز لانها لا تتعين فى المعاوضات فيتعلق بدراهم دين في الذمة فيجوز وعن ابي حنيفة رواه الحسن انه يجوز حتى اذا ملكه وسلمه يعتق فان عجز يرد في الرق لان المسمى مال والقدرة على التسليم موهومة فاشبه الصداق. قلنا ان العين في المعاوضة معقود عليه والقدرة على المعقود عليه شرط للصحة اذا كان العقد يحتمل الفسخ كما في البيع بخلاف الصداق في النكاح لان القدرة على ما هو المقصود بالنكاح ليس بشرط فعلى ما هو تابع فيه اولى
ترجمہ اسی طرح اگر اس کو مکاتب کیا معین طی پر جو دوسرے کی ہے تو جائز نہیں۔ کیونکہ غلام اس کی سپردگی پر قادر نہیں اور مراد ایسی چیز ہے جو معین ہو جاتی ہو تین سے یہاں تک کہ اگر کہا کہ میں نے تجھے ان ہزار درہموں پر مکاتب کیا حالانکہ وہ غیر کے ہیں تو جائز ہوگا۔ کیونکہ دراہم معاوضات میں متعین نہیں ہوتے تو عقد ایسے دراہم سے متعلق ہوگا جو ذمہ میں قرض ہوں گے۔ پس عقد جائز ہو جائے گا۔
He said: Likewise, if he makes him a mukatab for a specific thing belonging to another, it is not permissible, because he is not able to deliver it, and his intention is a thing that is determined by specification. So much so that if he said: 'I have made you a mukatab for these thousand dirhams' while they belong to another, it is permissible, because dirhams are not specified in commutative contracts, so they attach to dirhams as a debt in the dhimmah, so it is permissible. And from Abu Hanifa, al-Hasan narrated that it is permissible, to the extent that if he obtains it and delivers it, he becomes free; if he is unable, he returns to slavery, because the named item is property and the ability to deliver is hoped for, so it resembles the dower. We respond: The specific thing in commutative contracts is the subject of the contract, and ability to deliver the subject matter is a condition for validity when the contract is susceptible to rescission, as in sale. Unlike the dower in marriage, because ability to deliver what is the purpose of marriage is not a condition, so what is subsidiary is even more so not a condition.
Translation: Similarly, if he made him a mukatab for a specific item that belongs to another, it is not permissible, because the slave is not able to deliver it, and the intended item is determined by specification. So much so that if he said, 'I have made you a mukatab for these thousand dirhams' while they belong to another, it is permissible, because dirhams are not determined in commutative contracts, so the contract attaches to dirhams as a debt in the dhimmah, thus the contract is permissible.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling with an intra-Hanafi dispute. The general Hanafi position is that a kitabah contract with a specific item (ayn) owned by another is invalid (la yajuz) because the slave cannot deliver what he does not own. The ability to deliver the subject matter is a condition for validity in commutative contracts (mu'awadat) like sale, and kitabah is analogous. However, dirhams are an exception: even if specified as 'these thousand dirhams' belonging to another, the contract is valid because dirhams are not specified in commutative contracts; they become a debt (dayn) in the dhimmah. There is a narration from Abu Hanifa via Hasan ibn Ziyad that it is permissible for any specific item, as long as the slave later owns it and delivers it; if unable, he returns to slavery. The Hanafi majority refute this: in commutative contracts, ability to deliver at the time of contract is a condition, unlike in marriage where the dower is subsidiary and not subject to rescission. The relied-upon position rejects the permissive narration.

Key Terms
عین (ayn): A specific, identifiable item (e.g., a particular cloth or a specific sum of dirhams).
معاوضات (mu'awadat): Commutative contracts where each party gives and receives something of value (e.g., sale, kitabah).
صداق (sadaq): Dower given by husband to wife in marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi majority, what is the ruling if a master makes his slave a mukatab for a specific item (e.g., a cloth) that belongs to another?
A) Valid
B) Invalid
C) Valid only if the cloth is later delivered
D) Valid if the master guarantees it

Key Takeaway
In kitabah and other commutative contracts, ability to deliver the subject matter at the time of contract is a condition for validity; dirhams are an exception because they are not specified.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Mukatab and Muhabah: The Trade Custom Proof
Ruling that mukatab may sell at discount, with evidence from trade practice

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on muhabah by a mukatab and explain the urf-based proof used to support it.

Core Idea
A mukatab may sell at a discount because merchants commonly do so to profit overall—this is trade practice, not a gift.

A mukatab (a slave under a contract of manumission) needs to engage in trade to earn his freedom. Can he sell goods below market value? The Hanafi school says yes, based on the established practice of merchants. This lesson unpacks the ruling and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine exactly what act is being ruled upon: a mukatab selling at a price below market value (muhabah). The hukm is mubah (permissible).
2
Locate the dalil
The proof cited is trade custom (urf al-tujjar). The text states: 'because it is from the practice of merchants'.
This is a non-textual source (dalil aqli/urf).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does trade custom prove permissibility? The custom shows that discounting is a legitimate commercial strategy to secure overall profit. Since a mukatab is engaged in trade, he may employ the same strategies.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
There is intra-Hanafi disagreement: Abu Hanifa permits unconditionally; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad restrict it to conventional discounts. Also, other schools (Maliki, Shafi'i) consider it a gratuitous act (tabarru') like hibah.
The thamarah al-khilaf: under the opposing view, the mukatab cannot discount; under Hanafi, he can, but Sahibayn limit to moderate discounts.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The matn presents the unconditional ruling, and the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school is absolute permissibility according to Abu Hanifa.
This ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah.

And he is authorized to sell at a reduced price (muhabah) because it is from the practice of merchants; a merchant may give a discount in one transaction to profit in another.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage addresses the permissibility of a mukatab selling goods at a price below market value (muhabah). The proof is based on trade custom ('urf tujjar): merchants commonly discount one deal to secure overall profit. Since a mukatab needs to engage in trade to pay his kitabah, such transactions are permissible. The commentary (tashrih) notes that the three Imams (Malik, Shafi'i, and others) do not permit this, considering it a form of tabarru' (gratuitous transfer) akin to hibah. The Hanafi position, however, treats it as sound commercial practice. There is also an intra-Hanafi difference: Abu Hanifa permits it unconditionally, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) restrict it to what is conventionally accepted (لا يتغابن في مثله).

Key Terms
محاباة (muhabah): Selling at a price below the market value; giving a discount.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) A Quranic verse allowing discounts.
B) A hadith of the Prophet on trade.
C) The practice of merchants (urf al-tujjar).
D) Analogy to the master's permission.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits muhabah for a mukatab based on trade custom, distinguishing it from pure gifts.

Nature of Kitaba and Invalidity of Conditions
Clarifies resemblance of kitaba to itq and invalidates conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that an invalid condition in kitaba does not invalidate the contract, and distinguish the Hanafi position from the Shafi'i view.

Core Idea
Kitaba from the slave's side resembles manumission, so invalid conditions specific to the slave do not void the contract.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah resolves a key question: if Hanafis earlier argued that kitaba resembles marriage/manumission more than sale, why do they here apply a sale resemblance? The answer is that both analogies can operate in different contexts. More importantly, the passage provides the second proof that invalid conditions do not invalidate kitaba: kitaba from the slave's perspective resembles manumission, and like manumission, it is not nullified by invalid conditions. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and the inter-madhhab dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi ruling on the validity of kitaba when an invalid condition (e.g., 'not leaving Kufa') is attached.
The condition is void, but the kitaba remains valid.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Determine which school disagrees and on what grounds. Imam al-Shafi'i holds that kitaba resembles sale, so an invalid condition would nullify the contract.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the Shafi'i dalil: kitaba involves payment and transfer of ownership, making it like a sale contract.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Understand the Hanafi rebuttal: kitaba resembles manumission from the slave's side because both involve removal of ownership without transferring it to another. Conditions specific to the slave fall under manumission, not exchange.
The passage also notes that Hanafi scholars define manumission as establishment of subsidiary power, not mere removal.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical difference: under Hanafi law, the kitaba stands despite the void condition; under Shafi'i law, the condition might invalidate the entire contract.
This difference affects the slave's path to freedom.

تنبيه: صاحب ہدایہ کے قول تحبہ البیع و تشبہ النکاح پر یہ کہنے کی گنجائش ہے کہ غیر موصوف حیوان پر جو از کتابت کے مسئلہ میں ص ۱۳۵ پر یہ بات گذر چکی کہ ہمارے ائمہ نے مشابہت بالنکاح کا قول کر کے اس پر عمل کیا ہے اور امام شافعی نے جو مشابہت بالبیع کا قول کیا ہے۔ ہمارے ائمہ نے اس کی تردید کی ہے۔ جب یہ بات ہے تو پھر احناف کے لئے یہاں مشابہت بالبیع پر عمل کرنا کیسے صحیح ہوا۔ جواب یہ ہے کہ جس صورت میں شبین پر عمل ممکن ہو۔ اس میں دونوں پر عمل کرنا اس کے منافی نہیں کہ جہاں دونوں پر عمل ممکن نہ ہو وہاں صرف ایک پر عمل کیا جائے۔
قوله او نقول ... الخ - بطلان شرط و صحت کتابت کی دوسری دلیل یہ ہے کہ غلام کی جانب میں کتابت مشابہ اعتماق ہے۔ کیونکہ اعتاق ازالہ ملک لا الی واحد کو کہتے ہیں اور کتابت میں بھی اسقاط ملک ہے کیونکہ اس میں مکاتب کے پلے کچھ نہیں پڑتا صرف یہی ہوتا ہے کہ اس سے آقا کی ملک ساقط ہو جاتی ہے۔ اور شرط مذکور یعنی کوفہ سے باہر نہ جانا غلام کے ساتھ مخصوص یعنی اس سے متعلق ہے اور ہر وہ شرط جو مختص بجانب عبد ہو وہ داخل اعتاق ہوتی ہے تو شرط مذکور بھی داخل اعتاق ہوئی اس لئے اس شرط کے حق میں کتابت کو اعتاق اعتبار کیا گیا نہ کہ معاوضہ، اور اعتاق شروط فاسدہ سے باطل نہیں ہوتا۔ جیسے اگر کسی نے غلام اس شرط پر آزاد کیا کہ وہ سائبہ (چھٹیر ا) ہے تو یہ شرط باطل ہوگی اور اعتاق صحیح ہوگا۔
قوله لانه اسقط الملک الخ - اعتاق کا ساقط ملک ہونا ہمارے علماء کی تصریح کے خلاف ہے کہ اعتاق قوت فرعیہ کے اثبات کا نام ہے اور اس پر انہوں نے یہ بات متفرع کی ہے کہ اجنبی پر بدل محق کی شرط کرنا جائز نہیں۔ اگر اعتاق استفادط ملک ہوتا تو شرط جائز ہوتی کیونکہ اسقاطات میں اجنبی پر بدل کی شرط جائز ہے ہاں اثباتات میں جائز نہیں۔
N.B.: Regarding the statement of the author of Hidayah that [kitaba] resembles sale and resembles marriage, there is room to say that in the issue of kitaba on page 135, it had passed that our imams acted upon the view of resemblance to marriage, and Imam Shafi'i stated resemblance to sale. Our imams refuted that. When this is the case, then how for the Hanafis is it correct here to act upon resemblance to sale? The answer is that in a situation where it is possible to act upon both resemblances, acting upon both is not contradictory, for where it is not possible to act upon both, only one is acted upon. His statement: 'or we say...' - The second proof of the invalidity of the condition and the validity of kitaba is that from the slave's side, kitaba resembles manumission because manumission is the removal of ownership not to any particular [owner], and in kitaba there is also the removal of ownership because nothing falls into the possession of the mukatab; only the master's ownership is removed. And the mentioned condition, i.e., not leaving Kufa, is specific to the slave, i.e., it pertains to him, and every condition that is specific to the side of the slave enters into manumission. So the mentioned condition also entered into manumission. Therefore, with respect to this condition, kitaba is considered manumission, not an exchange, and manumission is not nullified by invalid conditions. For example, if someone freed a slave on the condition that he be a sa'ibah (a rogue), this condition is null and the manumission is valid. His statement: 'because he removed ownership' - [The idea that] manumission is the removal of ownership is contrary to the express statements of our scholars. Manumission is the establishment of a subsidiary power, and based on this they have derived that it is not permissible to stipulate a substitute against a stranger. If manumission were an acquisition of ownership, then such a condition would be permissible because in cases of removal, stipulating a substitute against a stranger is valid, whereas in cases of establishment it is not.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah deals with two interrelated points: the nature of kitaba (contract of manumission) regarding its resemblance to either sale or manumission, and the effect of invalid conditions on the validity of kitaba. First, the author addresses a potential inconsistency: earlier the Hanafis argued that kitaba resembles marriage (manumission) more than sale, and they refuted Imam Shafi'i's view of resemblance to sale. Yet here, in this context, they apply the resemblance to sale. The resolution is that when it is possible to apply both similarities, doing so is permissible; the contradiction only arises when both cannot be applied. Second, the passage provides the second proof that invalid conditions do not invalidate kitaba: kitaba from the slave's perspective resembles manumission. Both involve removal of ownership without transferring it to a specific party. Since conditions specifically related to the slave are part of manumission, the kitaba is treated as manumission regarding such conditions, and like manumission, it is not voided by invalid conditions. A key example is freeing a slave on the condition that he be a sa'ibah – the condition is invalid but the manumission stands. The author then notes a refinement from Hanafi scholars: manumission is not actually removal of ownership but rather establishment of a subsidiary power (quwwah far'iyyah). This distinction matters because if it were removal, a condition to pay compensation from a stranger would be valid; since it is establishment, such conditions are impermissible. This lesson underscores the Hanafi approach of using multiple analogies carefully and the robustness of kitaba against invalid conditions.

Key Terms
کتابت (kitābah): A contract between a master and his slave whereby the slave pays installments to earn his freedom.
اعتاق (i‘tāq): The act of freeing a slave; in this context, the removal of ownership (isqāt al-milk).
شرط فاسد (sharṭ fāsid): A condition that is invalid in Islamic law, such as one contradicting the contract's purpose.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the effect of an invalid condition (like not leaving Kufa) in a kitaba contract?
A) The kitaba becomes invalid.
B) The condition is void but the kitaba remains valid.
C) The condition is valid and must be fulfilled.
D) The kitaba becomes voidable at the option of the slave.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school treats kitaba as resembling manumission for conditions specific to the slave, thus such conditions are void but do not invalidate the contract.

Child of Mukatab's Umm Walad: Kitaba and Prohibition of Sale
Ruling on child entering kitaba and prohibition of selling mother

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two rulings when a mukatab purchases an umm walad: (1) the child enters the kitaba, and (2) the mother cannot be sold, and justify them using the Hanafi dalil and respond to the objection.

Core Idea
The child's eligibility for kitaba bars the sale of the mother due to her legal subordination to the child's right to freedom.

This passage from al-Hidayah presents a specific scenario: a mukatab purchases a slave woman who is an umm walad, i.e., she has borne a child to the mukatab. Two rulings emerge: the child automatically becomes part of the kitaba contract, and the mother cannot be sold. The author provides the reasoning and responds to an objection raised by Taj al-Shari'ah regarding the mother's status.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
What are the two legal consequences stated in the passage when a mukatab purchases an umm walad with his child?
One concerns the child, the other concerns the mother.
2
Understand the reason for the child entering kitaba
Why does the child become part of the kitaba? The text refers to the child being 'among those eligible for kitaba even if not eligible for emancipation.' What does that mean?
The child is a free person? Actually, the child of a mukatab from an umm walad is free from birth, but here it enters a contract.
3
Analyze the prohibition on selling the mother
What is the dalil (evidence) cited for the prohibition of selling the mother? How does the hadith 'Her child has set her free' apply?
The hadith is a proof that the mother is subordinate to the child in matters of freedom and sale.
4
Evaluate the objection and response
What objection does Taj al-Shari'ah raise? How does the Hanafi school resolve it?
The objection concerns the mother's right to freedom; the response clarifies that only specific rulings of kitaba transfer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جس کے ساتھ مکاتب کے نطفہ سے کوئی بچہ ہے۔ بہر کیف یہاں دو حکم مذکور ہیں ایک یہ کہ خرید ام ولد کی صورت میں بچہ داخل کتابت ہو جائے گا۔ دوسرے یہ کہ اب مکاتب اس کی ماں کو فروخت نہیں کر سکتا۔ بچہ کے داخل کتابت ہو جانے کی وجہ تو وہی ہے جو فصل کے آغاز میں گزر چکی انه من اهل ان يكاتب وان لم يكن من اهل الاعناق اٹھے اور اس کی ماں کو فروخت اس لئے نہیں کر سکتا کہ وہ حق آزادی اور عدم جواز بیع میں تابع ولد ہے کیونکہ حضور نے ارشاد فرمایا " اعتقها ولدها‘ اس کو اس کے بچہ نے آزاد کر دیا ۔ ( وقد تقدم في الاستيلاد)۔ پھرتاج الشریعہ نے یہاں یہ اعتراض ذکر کیا ہے کہ جب بچہ کے لئے حقیقی آزادی ثابت ہو جائے تو اس کی ماں کے لئے حق آزادی ثابت ہوتا ہے۔ اور یہاں چونکہ بچہ کے لئے حق آزادی ثابت ہوا ہے تو اس کی ماں کے لئے حق آزادی ثابت نہیں ہونا چاہیے تا کہ بچہ کی بہ نسبت ماں کے رتبہ کا انحطاط ظاہر ہو جائے۔ جواب یہ ہے کہ کتابت کے چند احکام ہیں جن میں سے ایک بیع کا جائز نہ ہونا ہے۔ پس ماں کے لئے یہ حکم ثابت ہوگا نہ کہ کتابت تحقيقاً لا نحطاط الرتبة.
With whom there is a child from the mukatab's sperm. In any case, two rulings are mentioned here: one is that when purchasing an umm walad, the child enters the kitaba. Second, that now the mukatab cannot sell her mother. The reason for the child entering the kitaba is what has passed at the beginning of the chapter: he is among those eligible for kitaba even if he is not eligible for emancipation. And he cannot sell his mother because she is subordinate to the child in the right of freedom and non-permissibility of sale, for the Prophet said: 'Her child has set her free.' (This has preceded in the chapter of istilad). Then Taj al-Shari'ah mentioned an objection here: when true freedom is established for the child, then the right of freedom is established for his mother. And here, since the right of freedom is established for the child, the right of freedom should not be established for his mother so that the inferiority of the mother's rank relative to the child becomes apparent. The answer is that kitaba has several rulings, among which is the non-permissibility of sale. So this ruling will be established for the mother, not kitaba itself, truly due to inferiority of rank.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof from the Hanafi school. It presents two related rulings: first, when a mukatab (a slave working under a manumission contract) purchases a slave woman who is an umm walad (mother of his child), that child automatically becomes part of the kitaba contract. Second, as a result, the mukatab cannot sell the mother. The justification for the child entering the kitaba is that the child, like the father, is eligible for such a contract. The prohibition on selling the mother is because she is subordinate to the child in matters of freedom and sale – the hadith 'Her child has set her free' (referring to the umm walad) establishes this subordination. The commentator Taj al-Shari'ah raises an objection: if the child attains a genuine right to freedom, the mother's right should be derivative, implying lower rank; but here the child only has a contingent right through kitaba, not full freedom. The response clarifies that the prohibition of sale is one specific ruling of kitaba, not the entire contract; the mother receives this ruling in a derivative manner without implying a complete transference of the kitaba status. Thus, the child's inclusion in the kitaba does not elevate the mother to a full kitaba status, but merely extends the prohibition of sale to her.

Key Terms
مکاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a kitaba (contract of manumission) with his master, agreeing to pay a sum or perform services in exchange for eventual freedom.
ام ولد (umm walad): A slave woman who has borne a child to her master, which child is free; she becomes free upon her master's death.
کتابت (kitaba): A contract between master and slave whereby the slave earns or pays money to become free, and the master cannot sell the slave during the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because she becomes free upon purchase
B) Because the child's right to freedom makes her subordinate in sale
C) Because the master's permission is required
D) Because kitaba automatically frees her

Key Takeaway
The child's eligibility for kitaba automatically integrates him into the contract and bars the sale of his mother due to her legal subordination to his right to freedom.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Sale of Umm Walad Without Child – Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the scope of umm walad status and the role of qiyas vs istihsan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the concepts of qiyas and istihsan to derive the Hanafi ruling on the sale of a umm walad by a mukatab, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
A mukatab cannot permanently attach the status of umm walad because his ownership is provisional.

This lesson explores a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute involving the interaction of mukatab (indentured servant) contracts, umm walad (mother of the master's child) status, and the interplay of qiyas and istihsan. The case is: a mukatab purchases a female slave who later gives birth to his child, but the child dies. Can he sell the mother? Imam Abu Hanifa and his two foremost students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), reach different conclusions, each based on a distinct method of legal reasoning. Understanding this dispute will sharpen your ability to discern the role of provisional ownership in the Hanafi school and the conditions under which istihsan overrides qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and the disputed ruling
The case: a mukatab's wife is actually his umm walad (she gave birth to his child, but the child is no longer with her). The question: can the mukatab sell her? The Sahibayn rule that she cannot be sold. Abu Hanifa rules that she can be sold.
The dispute is only when there is no child present. When a child is present, both agree the sale is not permissible (due to taba'iyyah).
2
Understand the Sahibayn's position and their dalil
The Sahibayn treat the woman as an umm walad in reality, regardless of the child's absence. They argue that she cannot be sold because her status is irrevocable.
Their basis is the general principle that an umm walad cannot be sold; the mukatab's incomplete ownership does not change her essential nature.
3
Understand Abu Hanifa's qiyas argument based on provisional ownership
Abu Hanifa employs qiyas: the mukatab's earnings (including his purchase of the woman) are mawquf (provisional) – they may revert to the master if he fails to pay. An irrevocable status like umm walad cannot attach to a provisional ownership.
The analogy: just as the mukatab's acts that require permanent ownership (like donations) are invalid, so too the status of umm walad cannot be permanently fixed on a woman he owns provisionally.
4
Recognize the exception when a child is present (istihsan via taba'iyyah)
If the child is present, the prohibition of sale is established for the child (who is in kitaba). The mother's prohibition is derived tab'an (subordinately) from the child, not independently. Hence, when no child exists, the derivative reason fails, and the original qiyas (permissibility) remains.
This shows that istihsan is applied only when the child is present; otherwise, the qiyasi ruling prevails.
5
Compare the two positions and determine the mufta bihi
The Sahibayn's view (sale not permissible) is more cautious and is generally relied upon in fatwa, though Abu Hanifa's view is also recorded. The practical effect: in most Hanafi contexts, a mukatab cannot sell his umm walad even without a child.
This dispute highlights how the Sahibayn gave weight to the reality of the situation (istihsan) over strict analogy (qiyas) in this case.

قوله وان لم يكن معها.... الخ - اور اگر مکاتب کی مذکورہ بیوی کے ساتھ مکاتب سے اس کا بچہ ساتھ نہ ہو تو صاحبین کے نزدیک اس کا حکم بھی یہی ہے کہ مکاتب اس عورت کو فروخت نہیں کر سکتا کیونکہ وہ عورت در حقیقت اس کی ام ولد ہے۔ اور امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک اس صورت میں اس کی بیع جائز ہے۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ مقتضائے قیاس تو یہی تھا کہ بچہ ساتھ ہونے کی صورت میں بھی اس کی بیع جائز ہو اس لئے کہ مکاتب کی کمائی بالفعل موقوف ہوتی ہے کہ اگر وہ بدل کتابت ادا کر دے تو آزاد ہو جائے گا اور جو کمائی اداء بدل سے بیچ رہے وہ اسی کی ہوگی۔ اور اگر وہ اداءِ بدل سے عاجز ہو جائے تو غلامی کی طرف پھر آئے گا اور جو کچھ اس کے پاس ہے وہ آقا کا ہوگا۔ اسی لئے بیوی کو خریدنے سے اس کا نکاح فاسد نہیں ہوتا۔ نیز اس کے تبرعات صحیح نہیں ہوتے ۔ معلوم ہوا کہ مکاتب کو ختمی ملک حاصل نہیں ہے بلکہ اس کی کمائی موقوف ہے اور ہر موقوف قابل فسخ ہوتا ہے تو مکاتب کی کمائی بھی قابل فسخ ہوئی اور جو چیز قابل فسخ ہو اس سے ایسا حکم متعلق نہیں ہوسکتا جو نا قابل فخ ہو جیسے استیلا د۔ اس لئے کہ اگر وہ اس سے متعلق ہو تو یا تو مکاتب کی کمائی غیر محتمل فسخ ہوگی یا استیلا تشتمل فسخ ہوگا۔ حالانکہ ان دونوں میں سے کوئی بھی جائز نہیں ہے۔ بہر کیف مقتضائے قیاس تو بچہ ہونے کی صورت میں بھی یہی ہے کہ اس عورت کی بیچ جائز ہو۔ لیکن بچہ ساتھ ہونے کی صورت میں امتناع بیع کا حکم ہم نے اس لئے ثابت کیا ہے کہ بچہ میں یہ حکم ثابت ہو کر اس کی بنا پر تبعا اس کی ماں میں یہ حکم ثابت ہو گیا ۔ اگر بچہ کے بغیر بھی یہ حکم ثابت کیا جائے تو ابتداء ہی سے مستقل طور پر ثابت ہوگا ۔ حالانکہ قیاس اس کی نفی کرتا ہے۔ پس بچہ کے بغیر حکم استحسانی ثابت نہ ہوگا ۔ بلکہ قیاسی حکم رہے گا کہ مکاتب اس کو فروخت کر سکتا ہے۔
His statement: 'And if she is not with...' etc. - And if the mukatab's aforementioned wife is not accompanied by his child from him, then according to the Sahibayn, the ruling is the same: the mukatab cannot sell this woman because she is in reality his umm walad. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, in this case her sale is permissible. The proof is that the requirement of qiyas was that even when accompanied by a child, her sale would be permissible because the mukatab's earnings are in abeyance: if he pays the kitaba price he becomes free, and the earnings he accumulates during payment are his; if he becomes unable to pay, he returns to slavery and whatever he has becomes the master's. For this reason, purchasing a wife does not invalidate his marriage, and his donations are not valid. It becomes known that the mukatab does not possess complete ownership; rather his earnings are in abeyance, and everything in abeyance is liable to annulment. So the mukatab's earnings are also liable to annulment, and from a thing liable to annulment, a ruling that is not liable to annulment—like istilad—cannot be attached. Because if it were attached, either the mukatab's earnings would become not liable to annulment or istilad would become liable to annulment, neither of which is permissible. Nevertheless, the requirement of qiyas even in the case with a child is that her sale is permissible. But in the case with a child, we have established the ruling of prohibition of sale because this ruling is established in the child, and by means of it, as a consequence, this ruling became established in his mother. If we were to establish this ruling without a child, it would be established initially and independently, whereas qiyas negates it. Therefore, without a child, the istihsani ruling will not be established; rather the qiyasi ruling will remain, which is that the mukatab can sell her.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute (ikhtilaf) between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) concerning a case where a mukatab's wife (who is actually an umm walad in reality) has no child from him. The Sahibayn hold that she cannot be sold, treating her as a genuine umm walad. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that her sale is permissible. The reasoning is based on qiyas (analogy): the mukatab's ownership of earnings is provisional (mawquf) – if he pays off his kitaba, they become his; if he fails, they revert to the master. Since his ownership is revocable, it cannot permanently attach the status of umm walad (which is irrevocable) to a woman. Therefore, the woman can be sold. However, when a child is present, the child himself becomes subject to kitaba, and the mother's prohibition of sale is established derivatively through the child (taba'iyyah). Without a child, this derivative reason is absent, so the original qiyas (permissibility of sale) prevails. Thus Abu Hanifa rejects istihsan here, while the Sahibayn apply istihsan based on the woman's reality as umm walad.

Key Terms
صاحبین (Sahibayn): Refers to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the two principal students of Abu Hanifa.
قیاس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning used to derive rulings for new cases based on existing ones.
استحسان (istihsan): A method of juristic preference that abandons strict analogy for a more equitable ruling.
ام ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master. She cannot be sold and becomes free upon the master's death.
مکاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered a contract (kitaba) with his master to purchase his freedom by paying a fixed sum.
تابعیت (taba'iyyah): The legal principle of subordination: a ruling established for a primary thing applies to its subordinate thing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the basis for Abu Hanifa's ruling that a mukatab can sell his umm walad when no child is present?
A) Because the mukatab's master grants permission.
B) Because the woman's status as umm walad is not fully established.
C) Because the mukatab's ownership of earnings is provisional and cannot support an irrevocable status.
D) Because the child's absence makes the sale a non-issue.

Key Takeaway
The sale of a mukatab's umm walad without a child is governed by qiyas (permissible according to Abu Hanifa) but the Sahibayn restrict it based on the reality of umm walad, and the mufta bihi is the Sahibayn's view.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Rulings on Mukatab's Intercourse with a Slave Woman: Ownership vs Marriage and Fasid Purchase
Explains rulings on 'aqr for mukatab's intercourse in ownership and marriage scenarios

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on 'aqr liability for a mukatab to distinguish between ownership-based and marriage-based intercourse, and state when the 'aqr is taken in the kitaba versus deferred.

Core Idea
The 'aqr for a mukatab's intercourse is due immediately if based on trade, but deferred if based on marriage.

The mukatab (slave under kitaba) has a unique status: he can own property and engage in trade, but remains under the master's ownership. This lesson unpacks a specific ruling from Al-Hidayah concerning liability for 'aqr (compensation) when a mukatab has intercourse with a slave woman. The key distinction is whether the intercourse occurred under a claim of ownership (e.g., purchase) or through marriage. The difference hinges on whether the act falls within the scope of trade, which is encompassed by the kitaba contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Note the two cases: ownership-based (purchase) and marriage-based. Observe the different rulings: for ownership, the 'aqr is taken immediately in the kitaba; for marriage, it is deferred until freedom.
The third scenario of fasid purchase is an extension of the ownership case.
2
Locate the 'illa (reason)
The text states that trade and its appendages are included in the kitaba, while marriage is not trade. The 'aqr is considered an appendage of trade because it drops the hadd (punishment) that would otherwise apply.
The 'illa is the inclusion in trade, not the nature of the debt itself.
3
Understand the linkage to the hadd
Without the purchase, the mukatab would be liable for the hadd of zina. The 'aqr substitutes for that hadd, thus it is tied to the trade transaction.
This linkage is key: the 'aqr becomes a trade-related liability.
4
Apply to fasid purchase
A fasid (invalid) purchase is still a form of trade, so the kitaba and authorization encompass both valid and invalid transactions, like in agency. Hence, the 'aqr is also immediate.
Fasid = invalid but still within the category of trade.
5
Note the condition 'without permission'
The matn includes 'without the master's permission' to indicate that with permission, the liability is even stronger (a fortiori). Also, if the slave is authorized for marriage, the 'aqr for marriage is immediate.
Permission from the master changes the ruling in the marriage case.
