Abu Hanifa on Co-owners' Liability in a Joint Makatibah Case
Ruling of Abu Hanifa on co-owners' liabilities in a joint makatibah case

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the liabilities of co-owners of a joint makatibah according to Abu Hanifa and evaluate the reasoning behind the liability for the child's value.

Core Idea
Istilad transfers ownership; the deceived co-owner pays full 'aqr and child's value, establishing lineage.

In this passage, we examine a detailed ruling of Imam Abu Hanifa on a scenario where two co-owners jointly own a slave-girl who is a makatibah. Each has intercourse with her at different times, and she gives birth. The first co-owner claims the child, triggering a series of compensatory payments. Abu Hanifa's reasoning balances the rights of the makatibah, the co-owners, and the child's status, including an apparent exception to the rule that an umm walad has no monetary value. Understanding this ruling deepens your grasp of istilad, shubha, and the Hanafi approach to competing claims.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the Parties and the Sequence of Events
Begin by noting the two co-owners: the first who claims the child (wati' awwal) and the second who also has intercourse later (wati' thani). The makatibah is jointly owned and under kitabah. Identify the key acts: intercourse, birth, and the first owner's claim of parentage.
The first co-owner completes istilad by claiming the child; the second co-owner acts under apparent ownership (shubha).
2
2. Determine the First Co-owner's Liabilities
The first co-owner must pay his partner half the price of the slave-girl (because he acquires the partner's share through complete istilad) and half the 'aqr (because the intercourse was with a jointly owned slave). Understand that liability for half price is tied to ownership transfer via istilad.
The first co-owner does not pay full 'aqr because his intercourse was partially justified by his ownership.
3
3. Determine the Second Co-owner's Liabilities
The second co-owner, though appearing to own a share at the time of intercourse, is treated as deceived (maghrur). He must pay the full 'aqr and the value of the child. The child is his son (lineage established) and free by paying its value. Analyze why he pays full 'aqr: because in reality, the slave-girl had become the first co-owner's umm walad.
The child's value is required even though Abu Hanifa generally holds that an umm walad is not subject to monetary compensation. This is a point of contention.
4
4. Address the Objection Regarding the Child's Value
The objection states that if an umm walad is not muttaqawwim (subject to valuation), then her child should not be valuable either. The commentary responds that there is a riwaya from Abu Hanifa that allows the child to be valued. Consider the strength of this response and the citation from Ibn al-Humam.
This illustrates how later scholars harmonize apparent contradictions within a madhhab.

Source Text

قوله و ايها دفع الخ. پھر شریکین میں سے جس نے مکاتبہ کو اس کے عاجز ہونے سے قبل عقر دے دیا جائز ہے۔ کیونکہ جب تک کتابت باقی رہے وصولی عقر کا حق خود مکاتبہ کو ہے اس لئے کہ اس کو اپنی ذات کے منافع اور معاوضات کا اختصاص حاصل ہے اور عاجز ہو جانے کی صورت میں عقر آقا کو اس لئے واپس دے گی کہ اب آقا کا اختصاص ظاہر ہو گیا۔ یہ مذکورہ کل تفصیل امام ابوحنیفہ کے یہاں ہے۔
The first co-owner is liable to his partner for half the price of the slave-girl because he acquired ownership of the partner's share upon complete istilad. He is also liable for half the 'aqr (dower) due to intercourse with a jointly owned slave-girl. His partner is liable for the full 'aqr and the value of the child, and the child is his son because he is like one deceived (maghrur): at the time of intercourse his ownership apparently existed, and the child of a deceived person is of confirmed lineage from him and free by value as is known. But he actually had intercourse with another's umm walad, so full 'aqr is obligatory on him. Whichever of them gives the 'aqr to the makatibah is permissible because as long as the kitabah remains, the right to take it belongs to her due to her exclusive right to her benefits and compensations. When she becomes unable (to pay), she returns the 'aqr to the master due to the emergence of his exclusive right. All this is the saying of Abu Hanifah.

(Urdu translation and commentary follow similar explanation with additional details and a note about the objection regarding the child's value and responses.)
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling of Imam Abu Hanifah regarding a case where two co-owners jointly own a slave-girl who is a makatibah (under kitabah). One of them (first co-owner) has intercourse with her, and she gives birth. The first co-owner claims the child. According to Abu Hanifah, the first co-owner becomes the owner of the entire slave-girl because the right to istilad (production of a child from the master) is completed for him, so he must pay his partner half the price of the slave-girl. Additionally, since the intercourse was with a jointly owned slave-girl, he must pay half the 'aqr (the standard dower). The second co-owner also has intercourse with her. Although initially his ownership was apparent, he actually had intercourse with a slave-girl who is now established as the first co-owner's umm walad. Therefore, he must pay the full 'aqr and the value of the child. However, the child is considered his son (established lineage) because he acted under a valid doubt (shubha) of ownership, and such a child is free by paying its value. The passage also discusses who has the right to receive the 'aqr: while the kitabah contract is in force, the makatibah herself receives it; if she fails to pay and returns to slavery, the 'aqr goes to the master. A note (tanbih) addresses an objection: if Abu Hanifah holds that an umm walad is not subject to monetary value (muttaqawwim), why is the second co-owner held liable for the child's value? The commentary provides multiple responses: some attribute this to the view of the two companions (Sahibayn), but the author criticizes this as contradictory to the explicit wording that all this is Abu Hanifah's view. A better response is that there are multiple riwayat (reports) from Abu Hanifah, and according to one such report, the child is considered muttaqawwim. The passage then returns to elaborating on the payment of 'aqr to the makatibah before her 'ajz (inability to pay).

Key Terms
مكاتبة (makatibah): A female slave who has entered into a kitabah contract (contract of emancipation upon payment of a sum).
عقر ('aqr): The standard dower (mahr al-mithl) that must be paid when a man has intercourse with a slave-girl who is not his property, or with a partially owned slave.
مغرور (maghrur): One who is deceived (e.g., in thinking a slave-girl is his property when she is not). Here, the second co-owner is treated as maghrur because he had apparent ownership.
استيلاد (istilad): The act of a master causing his female slave to give birth, which eventually makes her an umm walad.
متقوم (muttaqawwim): Having monetary value according to Islamic law; an object that can be sold and compensated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why must the first co-owner pay his partner half the price of the jointly owned makatibah?
A) Because he committed a sin by intercourse.
B) Because he acquired the partner's share through complete istilad.
C) Because the partner forced him to pay.
D) Because the makatibah requested it.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's ruling on joint makatibah establishes that istilad transfers ownership, the maghrur pays full 'aqr and child's value, and the child's value is justified by a riwaya even though the umm walad is generally not valued.

Sahibayn's View on Joint Makatibah and Paternity Claim
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad's ruling that the slave-girl becomes full umm walad for the first claimant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Sahibayn's ruling on a jointly owned makatibah when paternity is claimed and explain how partial annulment of kitabah is used to complete umm walad status.

Core Idea
Kitabah can be partially annulled to complete umm walad status; unlike tadbir or sale.

This passage presents the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn) in a classical Hanafi dispute with Imam Abu Hanifah. The case involves two co-owners of a makatibah (a slave under a kitabah contract). One co-owner claims paternity of her child. The Sahibayn rule that she becomes his full umm walad immediately, making the second co-owner's intercourse invalid. Their reasoning hinges on the revocable nature of kitabah, which allows partial annulment to achieve the umm walad status without nullifying the entire contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and the disputing imams
The case involves two co-owners of a makatibah. One claims her child. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ from Abu Hanifah.
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute.
2
State the Sahibayn's position
She becomes full umm walad for the first co-owner, the second's intercourse is void, no lineage for his child, no hadd, full 'aqr.
The second co-owner's child is not free by value.
3
Explain the dalil: partial annulment of kitabah
The status of umm walad must be completed if possible; kitabah is revocable, so it can be partially annulled to the extent needed, unlike tadbir or sale.
Partial annulment preserves the remaining kitabah obligations.
4
Contrast with Abu Hanifah's position
Abu Hanifah held that the first co-owner becomes owner of the other's share, and the second's child is his free son by value.
No partial annulment is needed under Abu Hanifah's view.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Sahibayn, the second child has no lineage and no value paid; under Abu Hanifah, the child is free and the father pays its value.
The practical difference in lineage and financial obligations.

وقال ابويوسف ومحمد هي ام ولد للاول ولا يجوز وطى الآخر لانه لما ادعى الاول الولد صارت كلها ام ولد له لان امومية الولد يجب تكميلها بالاجماع ما امكن وقد امكن بفسخ الكتابة لانها قابلة للفسخ فتفسخ فيما يتضرر به المكاتبة وتبقى الكتابة فيما وراءه بخلاف التدبير لانه لا يقبل الفسخ وبخلاف بيع المكاتب لان في تجويزه ابطال الكتابة اذ المشترى لا يرضى ببقائه مكاتبا واذا صارت كلها ام ولد له فالثاني واطىء ام ولد الغير فلا يثبت نسب الولد منه ولا يكون حرا عليه بالقيمة غير انه لا يجب الحد عليه للشبهة ويلزمه جميع العقر لان الوطى لا يعرى عن احد الغرامتين واذا بقيت الكتابة وصارت كلها مكاتبة له قيل يجب عليه نصف بدل الكتابة لان الكتابة انفسخت فيما لا يتضرر به المكاتبة ولا تتضرر بسقوط نصف البدل وقيل يجب كل البدل لان الكتابة لم تنفسخ الافى حق التملك ضرورة فلا يظهر في حق سقوط نصف البدل وفي ابقائه في حقه نظر للمولى وان كان لا يتضرر المكاتبة بسقوطه والمكاتبة هى التى تعطى العقر لاختصاصها بابدال منافعها ولو عجزت وردت في الرق يرد الى المولى لظهور اختصاصه على ما بينا
ترجمہ صاحبین فرماتے ہیں کہ وہ ام ولد ہے پہلے مدعی کی اور دوسرے کا ولی کرنا جائز نہیں ۔ کیونکہ جب پہلے شریک نے بچہ کا دعوی کیا تو وہ پوری اس کی اتم ولد ہو گئی ۔ اس لئے کہ ام ولد ہونے کی تکمیل بالا جماع ضروری ہے جہاں تک ممکن ہو اور یہاں مسیح کتابت کے ذریعہ سے ممکن ہے۔ کیونکہ کتابت قابل فسخ ہے پس اس کو فتح کر دیا جائے گا۔ اس امر میں جس میں مکاتبہ کو ضرر نہ پہنچے اور اسکے ماسوا میں باقی رکھا جائے گا بخلاف مدبر کرنے کے کہ یہ قابل فسخ نہیں ہے اور بخلاف مکاتب کی بیع کے کہ اس کے جائز رکھنے میں کتابت باطل کرنا ہے کیونکہ مشتری راضی نہ ہوگا اس کے مکاتب باقی رہنے پر اور جب پوری باندی اس کی ام ولد ہوگئی تو دوسرا شریک غیر کی اتم ولد سے وطی کرنے والا ہوا تو اس سے نسب ثابت نہ ہوگا۔ اور وہ اس پر بقیمت آزاد بھی نہ ہو گا۔ صرف اتنی بات ہے کہ اس پر حد واجب نہ ہوگی۔ شبہ کی وجہ سے اور اس کو پورا عقر لازم ہوگا۔ کیونکہ ولی کسی ایک تاوان سے خالی نہیں ہوتی ۔ اور جب کتابت باقی رہی اور وہ پوری اس کی مکاتبہ ہوگئی تو کہا گہا ہے کہ اس پر نصف بدل کتابت واجب ہوگا ۔ کیونکہ کتابت ایسے امر میں صلح ہوئی جس میں مکاتبہ کا ضرر نہ ہو اور نصف بدل ساقط ہونے میں...
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: She is an umm walad for the first (co-owner), and it is not permissible for the other to have intercourse. Because when the first claimed the child, she became entirely his umm walad. For the status of umm walad must be completed by consensus as much as possible, and it is possible by annulling the kitabah, because it is subject to annulment. So it is annulled to the extent that the makatibah is harmed, but the kitabah remains beyond that. Unlike tadbir (the contract of tadbir), which cannot be annulled, and unlike the sale of a makatib, because permitting that would nullify the kitabah since the buyer would not be content with him remaining a makatib. When she becomes entirely his umm walad, the second is having intercourse with another's umm walad, so lineage from him is not established, nor is the child free by his value. However, the prescribed punishment (hadd) is not required from him due to the doubt (shubha), and he is liable for the full 'aqr because intercourse is not devoid of one of the two penalties. If the kitabah remains and she becomes entirely his makatibah, it is said that he must pay half the kitabah price because the kitabah is annulled in a matter that does not harm the makatibah and she is not harmed by the fall of half the price. And it is said he must pay the full price because the kitabah was only annulled to the extent necessary for obtaining ownership, so it is not evident in the matter of halving the price; and keeping it in his favor is consideration for the master, even if the makatibah is not harmed by its fall. The makatibah is the one who gives the 'aqr due to her exclusive right to the compensations of her benefits; if she becomes unable and is returned to slavery, it is returned to the master due to the emergence of his exclusive right, as we have explained.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage presents the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two Sahibayn) on the same case. They differ from Abu Hanifah in that they consider the slave-girl to become fully umm walad for the first co-owner as soon as he claims the child, even though she was a makatibah. Their reasoning: the status of umm walad is to be fully achieved if possible, and here it is possible by annulling the kitabah contract partially. The kitabah is subject to annulment, unlike tadbir (a contract promising freedom after the master's death) which is irrevocable, and unlike selling a makatib, which would completely terminate the kitabah. So the kitabah is annulled only to the extent necessary to establish her umm walad status, but remains effective for other matters, such as the master's right to the writing price. Consequently, the second co-owner's intercourse is unlawful; his child does not have established lineage and is not free by value. However, due to the doubt (shubha) that his share was still his, the hadd punishment is not applied. He must pay the full 'aqr because his intercourse was with one who is not his property. Regarding the kitabah price, there is a dispute among the Sahibayn's own followers: some say the second co-owner must pay half the kitabah price (since the portion of the contract related to the first co-owner's share is annulled, reducing the price by half), while others say he pays the full price because the annulment was only for the purpose of establishing ownership, not to reduce the price. The makatibah receives the 'aqr as long as the kitabah continues; if she becomes unable to pay and returns to slavery, the 'aqr goes to the master.

Key Terms
ام ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne her master a child, thereby becoming free upon his death.
كتابة (kitabah): A contract between master and slave whereby the slave is promised freedom in exchange for a fixed payment over time.
تدبير (tadbir): A contract by which a master promises freedom to his slave upon the master's death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), what is the status of the second co-owner's child from the joint makatibah?
A) The child is his free son and he must pay its value.
B) The child is not established in lineage and no value is paid, but he pays full 'aqr.
C) The child is a slave for the first co-owner.
D) The child is free without any payment.

Key Takeaway
The Sahibayn allow partial annulment of kitabah to complete umm walad status, showing the flexibility of kitabah as a revocable contract.

Imam Muhammad's Ruling on Guarantee in Mukatabah
Explains Imam Muhammad's ruling on guarantee amount in mukatabah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Imam Muhammad's ruling on the guarantee amount for a co-owner in a mukatabah contract and explain the reasoning based on the principle that the lesser amount is certain.

Core Idea
The guarantee is the lesser of half the slave's value and half the remaining kitabah price.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah presents Imam Muhammad's specific ruling on how much a co-owner must guarantee when a mukatabah contract fails. The ruling hinges on identifying which of two possible rights is certain, ensuring the guarantor is not burdened with more than what is definitively owed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
We have a co-owner who becomes a guarantor for the other co-owner's share in a mukatabah contract. Determine the context: the mukatab may default or succeed in paying the kitabah.
2
Recognize the two possible rights
If the mukatab defaults, the co-owner's right is to half the slave (the slave regresses to slavery). If the mukatab pays, the right is to half the kitabah price. These are mutually exclusive possibilities.
3
Apply the principle of certainty
Since the actual outcome is uncertain at the time of guarantee, the co-owner's right is suspended between two possibilities. The lesser of the two values is taken as the wajib guarantee because it is certain (mutayyaqan) and doubt cannot overrun certainty.
This reflects the Hanafi legal maxim: 'What is certain is not removed by doubt.'
4
Compute the guarantee amount
Calculate half the slave's market value and half the remaining kitabah price. The guarantee is the lesser of these two amounts.
Example: if value = 1000 dirhams (half = 500) and remaining kitabah = 800 dirhams (half = 400), guarantee = 400 dirhams.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Muhammad's view differs from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. This passage focuses on his position; the other imams may calculate the guarantee differently.
Understanding this ikhtilaf is important for fatwa, as the mufta bihi position may vary by context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله وفي قول محمد .... الخ - اور امام محمد کے قول میں اس کی نصف قیمت اور باقی ماندہ نصف عوض کتابت میں سے جو کمتر ہو اس کا ضامن ہوگا۔ اس لئے کہ شریک کا حق دو اعتبار سے متعلق ہے ایک یہ کہ اگر وہ مکاتب اداء بدل کتابت سے عاجز ہو جائے تو اس کا حق نصف رقبہ ہے اوراگر وہ بدل کتابت ادا کر دے تو اس کا حق نصف عوض ہے۔ پس شریک کا حق متر در بین الاعتبار بین ہونے کی وجہ سے جم کمتر ہو وہ واجب ہوگا کیونکہ ان میں سے جو اقل ہے وہ متیقن ہے۔
He said: 'And in the view of Muhammad...' - In Imam Muhammad's view, he will be guarantor for the lesser of its half price and the remaining half of the kitabah price. Because the partner's right is related in two ways: one, if the mukatab becomes unable to pay the kitabah price, his right is to half the slave; and if he pays the kitabah price, his right is to half the price. So the partner's right is suspended between two considerations, so the lesser is wajib because the lesser is certain.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This is a ruling from Imam Muhammad concerning the situation where one co-owner of a mukatabah becomes responsible for the other co-owner's share. The ruling determines that the guarantee amount is the minimum of two possible values: half the market value of the slave if she regresses to slavery, or half the remaining kitabah amount if she becomes free. The reasoning is that the co-owner's claim alternates between these two possibilities upon the mukatabah's inability to pay, so the lesser amount is taken as the certain (mutayyaqan) minimum.

Key Terms
مکاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered a contract of kitabah to earn freedom by paying installments
عوض کتابت (iwad al-kitabah): The agreed price or compensation which the mukatab must pay to the master for emancipation
ضامن (damin): A guarantor who is liable to pay a specific amount to the co-owner

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad, what amount does the co-owner guarantee when the mukatabah becomes unable to pay?
A) Half the slave's market value
B) Half the remaining kitabah price
C) The lesser of half the value and half the remaining kitabah price
D) The full value of the slave

Key Takeaway
Imam Muhammad's ruling on guarantee amount in mukatabah is based on the principle that the lesser of two possible rights is certain and thus wajib.

Effect of Tadbir by Co-owner on Mukatabah and Consequences
Rules on tadbir by second co-owner and subsequent inability to pay

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of tadbir by a second co-owner of a mukatabah after the first co-owner's istilad, and explain the intra-Hanafi reasoning leading to the same outcome.

Core Idea
Tadbir requires full ownership; istilad by one co-owner retroactively vests ownership, rendering tadbir invalid.

This passage from al-Hidayah examines a nuanced case involving a jointly-owned mukatabah. When the first co-owner has intercourse with her (istilad) and the second subsequently makes her mudabbara (tadbir), her inability to pay the kitabah triggers a legal analysis. The Hanafi masters agree on the outcome—the tadbir is invalid and she becomes umm walad—but differ on the timing of ownership transfer. Understanding this case sharpens your ability to apply principles of ownership and the intersection of istilad and tadbir.

Guided Path
1
Define the scenario
Understand that two co-owners have a mukatabah slave girl; the first has intercourse, the second makes her mudabbara; she later cannot pay.
The kitabah is a contract of gradual emancipation, so the slave is partially owned.
2
Apply the Sahibayn’s reasoning
They hold that istilad transfers full ownership immediately, so the second's tadbir occurs when she is owned by the first, invalidating it.
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: ownership at the moment of intercourse.
3
Apply Abu Hanifa’s reasoning
He holds that ownership transfers retroactively from the time of intercourse, revealed by her inability, so the second lacked ownership at the time of tadbir.
Abu Hanifa: ownership is established retroactively upon inability.
4
Identify the consensus on outcome
Despite the ikhtilaf, all agree that she becomes umm walad for the first, compensation is due (half value and half 'aqr), and the child's lineage is established.
The ikhtilaf does not affect the final ruling because the kitabah is terminated.
5
Note the scope of ikhtilaf
The disagreement only matters when the kitabah remains; here it is cancelled, so the ruling is unanimous.
The text explicitly states 'this is the view of all of them'.

The second [co-owner] did not have intercourse with her but made her mudabbara, then she became unable to pay, so the tadbir becomes invalid. He said: 'And if the second did not have intercourse with her but made her mudabbara, then she becomes unable to pay, the tadbir is invalid because it did not coincide with ownership. According to them [the Sahibayn], it is apparent because the one who caused istilad owned her before the inability. According to Abu Hanifa, because the inability shows that he owned his share from the time of intercourse, so it becomes clear that it coincided with the ownership of another. And tadbir relies on ownership, unlike lineage, which relies on deception as previously mentioned. He said: 'She is an umm walad for the first because he owned his partner's share and istilad was completed as we explained. He guarantees to his partner half her 'aqr for his intercourse with a jointly-owned slave girl and half her value, because he owns half of her through istilad, and that is ownership by value. The child is the child of the first because his claim of lineage is correct due to the existence of the correctifier, and this is the view of all of them, and its explanation is as we have explained.' Translation: And if the second did not have intercourse with her but made her mudabbara, then she became unable to pay, the tadbir is invalid because it did not connect to ownership. According to the Sahibayn, it is apparent because the one who made her umm walad owned her before the inability. According to Abu Hanifa, because the inability shows that he owned the partner's share from the time of intercourse, so it becomes clear that the tadbir connected to the ownership of another, while tadbir relies on ownership, unlike lineage which relies on deception. And the slave girl is an umm walad for the first claimant because he owned his partner's share and istilad is complete. And he guarantees half her 'aqr for his intercourse with a jointly-owned slave girl, and half her value, because he owned half the slave girl through istilad, and that ownership is by value. And the child is the child of the first claimant because his claim of lineage is correct due to the existence of the correctifier, and this is the view of all of them. Explanation: And if the second partner did not have intercourse with her but made her mudabbara, then the slave girl became unable to pay the kitabah price, the tadbir becomes invalid. Because it did not connect to ownership – according to the Sahibayn it is apparent because the partner who made her umm walad owned her before the inability, so the kitabah was cancelled before the tadbir, hence the tadbir is not valid. And according to Imam Abu Hanifa, because the slave girl's inability shows that the first co-owner owned the share of the second from the time he had intercourse, so the second's making her mudabbara connected to the ownership of another, while tadbir is only valid when it is one's own property; if it is not one's own, tadbir is not valid. As for lineage, it is valid even without ownership – that is, if deception occurs, lineage is established by reliance on deception. And the slave girl is an umm walad for the first because he owned his partner's share and istilad is complete. And he will be guarantor to the second for half the price and half the dower. The guarantee for half the price is because he owned half the slave girl through istilad, and the guarantee for half the 'aqr is because he had intercourse with a jointly-owned slave girl. And the child that is born is the child of the first co-owner because his claim of lineage is correct, meaning the cause for the correctness of the claim of lineage exists, and that is ownership in the mukatabah, because his istilad occurred at the time of the kitabah contract. And the istilad of a mukatabah is valid by consensus. The author of Hidayah says that the above ruling is the view of all of them because the difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn is in the case where the kitabah remains, and here the kitabah did not remain. If both made her a mukatabah, then one freed her while he is wealthy, then the slave girl became unable to pay, the freer guarantees half her value to his partner, and he will recover that from the slave girl.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage deals with a scenario where two co-owners jointly make their slave girl a mukatabah. The first co-owner has intercourse with her, making her an umm walad. The second co-owner then makes her mudabbara (postponed emancipation) without intercourse. Subsequently, the slave girl becomes unable to pay the kitabah installments. The ruling is that the tadbir is invalid. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) reason that the first co-owner owned her completely before the inability because his istilad gave him full ownership of her. Abu Hanifa holds that the inability retroactively reveals that the first co-owner owned the second's share from the moment of intercourse, so the tadbir intersected with non-ownership. Despite this ikhtilaf, all agree that she becomes an umm walad for the first co-owner, he must compensate the second with half her value and half the 'aqr (dower for intercourse with a slave), and the child's lineage is established to the first co-owner. The passage ends by noting that the ikhtilaf only applies when the kitabah remains, but here it is terminated by inability.

Key Terms
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave declared to be free upon the master's death
استیلاد (istilad): The act of a master having intercourse with a slave woman, making her an umm walad
عقر (aqr): Compensation (dower) for intercourse with a jointly-owned slave woman
ام ولد (umm walad): A slave woman who has borne a child to her master, entitling her to freedom upon his death

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, why is the tadbir invalid in this case?
A) Because the second co-owner did not have intercourse
B) Because the first co-owner had already owned the slave completely before the inability
C) Because the slave was a mukatabah
D) Because the tadbir was done after inability

Key Takeaway
The tadbir of a co-owned mukatabah becomes invalid when the other co-owner's istilad establishes ownership, and the child is attributed to the first co-owner.

Recovery after Default: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn on Divisibility of Freeing
Intra-Hanafi dispute on recovery from slave after freeing by one co-owner

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn differ on the right to recover from the slave after default, and identify the underlying principle of tujazzi' al-i'taq.

Core Idea
The divisibility of freeing determines who bears the loss when a freed mukatabah defaults.

A jointly owned mukatabah is freed by one co-owner while he is wealthy. Later, she defaults on her kitabah payments. Should the freeing co-owner compensate his partner and then recover from the slave? The answer hinges on the Hanafi school's internal dispute on whether freeing is divisible. This lesson explores the legal reasoning of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and how their disagreement on tujazzi' al-i'taq leads to opposite conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual scenario
Two co-owners jointly own a mukatabah (a slave who contracts to earn her freedom by paying installments). One co-owner frees her while he is wealthy. She then defaults on her payments and is returned to slavery.
2
State the agreed ruling
All agree that the freeing co-owner must guarantee half the slave's value to his partner as compensation. This is wajib.
The dispute is not about the guarantee itself.
3
Identify the disputed issue
After paying the partner, can the freeing co-owner recover the amount he paid from the slave (now that she is again a slave)?
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's reasoning
For Abu Hanifa, freeing is divisible. The freeer's action only frees his own share. The partner's share remains that of a mukatabah. When she defaults, the partner can claim his share's value from the freeer, and the freeer then has recourse against the slave.
Key principle: tujazzi' al-i'taq.
5
Analyze the Sahibayn's reasoning
For the Sahibayn, freeing is indivisible. The entire slave becomes free. When she defaults and is returned to slavery, it is as if she never ceased being a slave (retroactive effect). Therefore, she cannot be charged for the value again.
The slave's liability is erased by the retroactive return to slavery.
6
Connect to the broader principle
The dispute is a direct application of the principle of tujazzi' al-i'taq. The ruling for other related issues (e.g., options, recourse) follows the same logic.
See the chapter on freeing for more details.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the state of wealth and poverty: He said: And if both made her a mukatabah, then one freed her while he is wealthy, then she became unable to pay, the freer guarantees half her value to his partner, and he recovers that from her according to Abu Hanifa. They (the Sahibayn) said: he does not recover from her, because when she became unable and was returned to slavery, it is as if she never ceased being a slave. The answer in this is based on the difference in recourse and in options etc., just as it is the issue of divisible freeing, which we have established in the chapter on freeing. As for before inability, the partner does not have the right to make the freer guarantee according to Abu Hanifa, because when freeing is divisible in his view, its effect is that the share of the non-freer becomes like a mukatab, so his share does not change because she was a mukatabah before. According to them (the Sahibayn), because freeing is not divisible, the whole becomes free, so he can make him guarantee the value of his share as a mukatabah if he is wealthy, and he can seek payment from the slave if he is poor, because it is a guarantee for freeing, so it differs based on wealth and poverty. The Sahibayn say: he will not recover from her, because when she became unable and was made a slave again, it is as if she was always a slave. And the answer in this is based on the difference regarding recourse and options etc., as it is the issue of divisible freeing, which we have explained in the chapter on freeing. As for before inability, the first partner does not have the option to take compensation from the freer according to Imam Abu Hanifa, because when freeing is divisible in his view, its effect is that the share of the non-freer becomes like a mukatab, so his share does not change because she was a mukatabah before. According to the Sahibayn, since freeing is not divisible, the whole becomes free, so he can make him guarantee the value of his share as a mukatabah if he is wealthy, and he can seek payment from the slave if he is poor, because it is a guarantee for freeing, so it differs based on wealth and poverty.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a jointly-owned mukatabah whom one co-owner frees while wealthy, after which she defaults on the kitabah payments. Abu Hanifa rules that the freeing co-owner must guarantee half the slave's value to his partner, and then can recover that amount from the slave now that she has regressed to slavery. The Sahibayn hold that he cannot recover from the slave because her return to slavery erases the previous transaction; it is as if she was always a slave. The difference stems from the principle of tujazzi' al-i'taq (divisibility of freeing). Abu Hanifa considers freeing divisible, so the freeer's action only frees his own share; the partner's share remains as a mukatabah. Thus, after default, the partner can seek the value from the freeer, who then has recourse against the slave. The Sahibayn consider freeing indivisible, so the entire slave becomes free; the freeer must compensate the partner for his share immediately (if wealthy) or the slave must work to pay it (if poor). The passage also notes that before default, if the partner tries to claim compensation from the freeer, Abu Hanifa would disallow it because the partner's share is unaffected, while the Sahibayn would allow it.

Key Terms
تجزّی الاعتاق (tujazzi' al-i'taq): The divisibility of freeing; whether freeing can apply to only part of a slave
سعایت (si'ayah): Seeking payment from a slave through labor or earnings
ضمان اعتاق (daman i'taq): Guarantee for the act of freeing, leading to compensation to the co-owner

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the underlying reason for the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the freer's right to recover from the slave after default?
A) Whether the freer was wealthy or poor at the time of freeing.
B) Whether the slave was a mukatabah or an ordinary slave.
C) Whether freeing is divisible (tujazzi' al-i'taq) or not.
D) Whether the default occurred before or after the guarantee was paid.

Key Takeaway
The right to recover from a defaulted mukatabah after one co-owner frees her depends on whether freeing is divisible (Abu Hanifa) or indivisible (Sahibayn).

Child of a Deceased Mukatab: Born vs Purchased
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the obligation of a purchased child to pay installments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a child born during kitabah and distinguish it from the ruling for a child purchased during kitabah, explaining Abu Hanifa's argument against the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
A child born during kitabah is included in the contract; a child purchased is not—so the installment term does not apply.

When a mukatab (a slave with a manumission contract) dies before completing his payments, the fate of his children depends on whether they were born or purchased during the kitabah. The Hanafi school agrees on the ruling for a born child but splits on the purchased child. This lesson unpacks the dispute, focusing on the key legal principle that distinguishes the two cases.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling for a child born during kitabah
The mukatab dies without sufficient wealth but leaves a child born during the kitabah. The child must work (sa'a) to pay the father's installments. Upon completion, both father and child are deemed free retroactively.
This ruling is undisputed in the Hanafi school.
2
Identify the dispute for a child purchased during kitabah
If the child was purchased (not born) during the kitabah, Abu Hanifa rules the child must pay the entire kitabah immediately or be enslaved. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) analogize to the born child, allowing installment payments.
Imam Malik aligns with the Sahibayn.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's proof
The term (ajal) is a condition in the contract and only applies to those who entered under the contract. The purchased child did not enter the contract (he was bought later), so no term applies to him.
Key term: munfasil (separate) vs muttasil (connected).
4
Analyze the Sahibayn's proof and Hanafi response
The Sahibayn argue by qiyas: the purchased child is a dependent mukatab (makatab taba'an) like the born child. Abu Hanifa counters: the purchased child is separate from the original contract, whereas the born child was connected at the time of the contract, so the contract's ruling applies to him.
The common 'illa (effective cause) in qiyas is rejected due to the difference in connection.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and practical consequence
The relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's: the purchased child must pay immediately or be enslaved. The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is whether the child benefits from the installment period or not.
The commentary (Ashraf al-Hidaya) supports Abu Hanifa's distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

میں پیدا شدہ فرزند پر قیاس کرتے ہوئے اور امر جامع یہ ہے کہ وہ بھی مکاتب ہے میت پر تبعا اسی لئے آقا اس کو آزاد کرنے کا مالک ہوتا ہے بخلاف اس کی دیگر کمائیوں کے امام ابوحنیفہ کی دلیل اور وہی فرق ہے دونوں فصلوں میں یہ ہے کہ میعاد ثابت ہوتی ہے عقد میں شرط ہو کر تو یہ ہر اس کے حق میں ثابت ہوگی جو تحت العقد داخل ہوا ہو ۔ حالانکہ خریدا ہوا داخل نہیں ہوا۔ کیونکہ نہ عقد اس کی طرف مضاف ہے نہ عقد کا حکم اس تک ساری ہے اسکے الگ ہونے کی وجہ سے بخلاف کتابت میں پیدا شدہ کے کہ وہ کتابت کے وقت متصل ہے تو حکم اس تک سرایت کر گیا۔ اور جب وہ حکم کتابت میں داخل ہو تو باپ کی اقساط پر سعایت کرے گا اگر اپنے بیٹے کو خرید لیا پھر مر گیا اور بدل کے لائق مال چھوڑ گیا تو اس کا بیٹا اس کا وارث ہوگا۔ اس لئے کہ جب اس کی زندگی کے آخری جزء میں اس کی آزادی کا حکم دے دیا گیا تو اسی وقت سے اس کے بیٹے کی آزادی کا بھی حکم دیا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ وہ کتابت میں اپنے باپ کا تابع ہے پس یہ ایک آزاد ہوا جس نے آزاد کی میراث پائی۔ اسی طرح اگر وہ اس کا بیٹا دونوں ایک ہی کتابت میں مکاتب ہوں اس لئے کہ بیٹا اگر صغیر ہوتو وہ اپنے باپ کا تابع ہوا اور اگر بالغ ہوتو دونوں بمنزلہ ایک شخص کے قرار دئیے جائیں گے۔ پس جب باپ کی آزادی کا حکم دیا گیا تو اسی حالت میں بیٹے کی آزادی کا بھی حکم ہوگا جیسا کہ گذر چکا۔ تشریح قوله وان ترک ولداً مشترى الخ- ایک مکاتب غلام کا انتقال ہو اور اس نے وہ اولادچھوڑی جو کتابت کی حالت میں خریدی تھی تو امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک اولاد سے کہا جائے گا کہ یا تو فوراً کتابت ادا کرو ورنہ غلام ہو جاؤ۔ صاحبین اور امام مالک کے یہاں ان کا حکم بھی اسی اولادکا سا ہے جو کتابت کی حالت میں پیدا ہوئی ہو۔ یعنی وہ اپنے باپ کی قسطوں کے مطابق بدل کتابت ادا کریں گے۔ پس صاحبین نے ولد مشتری کو ولد مولود پر قیاس کیا ہے اور قیاس کی علت مشترکہ یہ ہے کہ دونوں تبعا مکاتب ہوتے ہیں چنانچہ خریدا ہوا فرزند بھی مکاتب میت پر بے اختیار مکاتب ہو گیا اسی لئے آقا کو یہ اختیار ہوتا ہے کہ اگر وہ چاہے تو اس کو آزاد کر دے بخلاف اس کی دیگر کمائیوں کے کہ آقا ان میں تصرف نہیں کر سکتا یہاں تک کہ اس کے خریدے ہوئے غلاموں کو آزاد نہیں کر سکتا۔
قوله ولا بي حنیفة ... الخ - امام ابوحنیفہ کی دلیل۔ جس سے ولد مشتری اور ولد مولود میں فرق بھی ظاہر ہو جاتا ہے یہ ہے کہ تا جبیل اس وقت ثابت ہوتی ہے جب عقد میں اس کی شرط ہو اور اسی کے حق میں ثابت ہوتی ہے جو تحت العقد داخل ہو اور خرید کردہ اولاد تحت العقد داخل نہیں کیونکہ نہ تو ان کی طرف عقد کی اضافت ہے اور نہ ان تک حکم عقد کی سرایت ہے بخلاف اس اولاد کے جو کتابت کی حالت میں پیدا ہوئی ہو کہ وہ بوقت کتابت مکاتب کے ساتھ متصل تھی اس لئے حکم عقدان تک سرایت کر گیا۔ اس لئے وہ باپ کی قسطوں کے مطابق ادا کرے گی۔
قوله فان اشترى ابنه..... الخ - اور اگر مکاتب نے اپنے بیٹے کو خرید لیا پھر مکاتب مرگیا اور اداء بدل کتابت کے لائق مال چھوڑ گیا تو اداء بدل کتابت کے بعد باقی ماندہ مال کا وارث اس کا بیٹا ہوگا۔ اس لئے کہ جب مکاتب کی حیات کے آخری لمحات میں اس کے آزاد ہونے کا حکم دے دیا گیا تو اسی وقت سے اس کے بیٹے کے آزاد ہونے کا بھی حکم دیا جائے گا کیونکہ بیٹا کتابت میں اپنے باپ کا تابع ہے تو یہ ایسا ہوا کہ آزاد بیٹے نے آزاد باپ کی میراث پائی۔ قوله وكذلك ان كان .... الخ - اسی طرح اگر مکاتب اور اس کا بیٹا دونوں ایک ہی کتابت میں مکاتب ہوں تب بھی یہی حکم
Book of the Mukatab. The answer is correct, but the agent's action is attributed to the principal, and this attribution is legal (shar'i). Similarly, if someone shoots an arrow at game and the archer dies before the arrow strikes, then the game belongs to him even though the deceased is not capable of ownership. Since the cause (sabab) was valid, ownership is established by being traced back to the last moments of his life. Similarly here: if he did not leave wealth but left a child born during the kitabah, the child will work (sa'a) to pay his father's kitabah on its installments. When he pays, we will rule that the father was freed before his death, and the child is also freed because the child is included in his kitabah, and the child's earnings are like his earnings, so he succeeds him in payment, and it is as if he left wealth. Explanation: If the mukatab did not leave utilization sufficient to pay the kitabah, but left a child born during the kitabah, that child will work according to his father's installments. After payment, it will be ruled that the father was freed before death and the child is freed because he is included in the kitabah. If he left a child purchased during the kitabah, he will be told: either pay the kitabah immediately or be enslaved according to Abu Hanifa. According to them (the two companions), he will pay on its term, analogizing to the child born during kitabah, and the common point (jam'i) is that he is a mukatab as a dependent (taba'an) on him, so the master owns his emancipation unlike other earnings. For Abu Hanifa, the difference between the two cases is that the term (ajal) is established as a condition in the contract, so it is established for the one who enters under the contract; the purchased child did not enter because the contract is not attributed to him, nor does its ruling apply to him due to his separation, unlike the born child who was connected at the time of the contract, so the ruling applied to him. Thus he works on his installments. If he purchased his son and then died leaving sufficient wealth, his son inherits from him because when we rule his freedom in the last part of his life, we also rule his son's freedom at that time because the son is dependent on his father in kitabah, so this is a free person inheriting from a free person. Similarly, if he and his son were both mukatab in one kitabah, because if the son is young he is dependent on his father; if adult they are treated as one person; so when freedom of the father is ruled, freedom of the son is also ruled in that state as before. Explanation: If a mukatab slave dies and leaves children purchased during kitabah, according to Abu Hanifa they will be told: either pay the kitabah immediately or become slaves. According to the two companions and Imam Malik, their ruling is the same as a child born during kitabah: they will pay the kitabah according to the father's installments. The two companions analogize the purchased child to the born child, and the common 'illa is that both become mukatab derivatively, so the master has the option to emancipate them, unlike other earnings. Abu Hanifa's proof: The term is established only when it is a condition in the contract and for those under the contract; purchased children did not enter because the contract is not attributed to them and its ruling does not apply to them due to their separation, unlike born children who were connected at the time of kitabah so the contract's ruling applied to them. Therefore the born child pays on installments. If the mukatab purchased his own son then died leaving sufficient wealth, the son inherits the remaining wealth after payment because when the father's freedom is ruled at the last moment of life, the son's freedom is also ruled at that time since the son is dependent on his father in kitabah, so the free son inherits from the free father. Similarly, if both are mukatab in one kitabah, the same ruling applies.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Hidayah discusses the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the obligation of a child left by a deceased mukatab (a slave with a manumission contract) to pay the father's outstanding installments. The text first establishes the ruling for a child born during the kitabah: such a child is included in the father's contract, and upon the father's death without sufficient wealth, the child must work to pay off the installments. Once paid, the father is deemed free from the moment before his death, and the child also becomes free, as the child's earnings are treated as the father's for this purpose. This is analogous to the case where the father left enough wealth. The ruling for a purchased child differs: Abu Hanifa holds that the purchased child, having not been part of the original contract, does not benefit from the payment term. Therefore, the child must either pay the entire kitabah immediately or be enslaved. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), however, analogize this to the born child, arguing that because the purchased child is a dependent mukatab (makatab taba'an), he should also be allowed to pay on the original installment plan. Abu Hanifa counters by pointing out that the term (ajal) in the contract is a condition established only for those who entered into the contract; the purchased child did not enter (he was bought later), so the term does not apply to him. Further, the passage illustrates an additional scenario: if the mukatab purchased his own son (who was already a slave separate from the father's kitabah) and then died with enough wealth to pay the kitabah, the son inherits the father's remaining estate because both become free retroactively at the father's death. The son's freedom follows from his being a dependent in the kitabah (taba'an), so he is considered free at the same moment as the father and can inherit from him. Similarly, if father and son are joint mukatab in a single contract, the same inheritance rule applies. This passage highlights the importance of whether a child was included in the original contract or not, and how the Hanafi school resolves such issues through careful distinction between inclusion by birth versus acquisition.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a contract (kitabah) with his master to purchase his freedom by paying a specified sum in installments.
كتابة (kitabah): The contract of manumission between a slave and his master, stipulating a price and schedule for payment.
سعى (sa'a): To work diligently or earn income to pay off the kitabah installments; it implies continuous effort to generate earnings.
تبعا (taba'an): As a dependent follower; a child who is automatically included in the father's kitabah by virtue of being connected to him at the time of the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a mukatab dies leaving a child purchased during the kitabah but no wealth, what must the child do?
A) Pay the entire kitabah immediately or be enslaved.
B) Pay the kitabah on the same installments as the father.
C) Work only to pay off half the kitabah.
D) Inherit the father's debt obligation.

Key Takeaway
The installment term (ajal) in kitabah is a contractual condition that does not extend to a child purchased after the contract, only to a child born during it.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Ownership Transfer and the Halal Nature of Sadaqah for the Master
Specifying the ruling of sadaqah becoming halal for the master when mukatab becomes unable

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a mukatab gives sadaqah to his master for kitabah and identify the hadith proof and its legal implication (wajh al-dalalah).

Core Idea
Change of ownership changes the ruling: sadaqah becomes halal for the master when taken as kitabah payment.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in the Kitab al-Mukatab: a slave under kitabah takes sadaqah and gives it to his master as part of the payment. When the mukatab later becomes unable to pay the remainder, is that sadaqah halal for the master? The Hanafi ruling, supported by a famous hadith, says yes—because the ownership of the item changed. We will walk through the ruling, its proof, and the subtle intra-Hanafi difference regarding the exact mechanism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine which act is being ruled upon: the master receiving sadaqah from the mukatab as payment for kitabah. State the hukm: it is halal (permissible) for the master.
The ruling hinges on the change of ownership, not on the inherent nature of sadaqah.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the evidence cited: the hadith of Barira (from Aisha) where the Prophet said meat given as sadaqah to Barira was sadaqah for her but a gift for him.
The hadith is narrated by Bukhari and Muslim (al-Shaykhayn).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Analyze how the hadith proves the ruling: the same meat changed its category when ownership transferred. The Prophet applied the principle that change of ownership changes the legal status.
This principle is explicit: 'tabaddul al-milk ka tabaddul al-ayn' (change of ownership is like change of the thing itself).
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Identify the difference between Imam Muhammad and Imam Abu Yusuf. Imam Muhammad holds that ownership changes immediately upon the mukatab's inability; Imam Abu Yusuf says it does not change but the sadaqah is still halal because the defect is in taking, not in the essence.
Both agree on the outcome (halal), but the rationale differs.
5
Apply the ruling to similar cases
Consider the contrasts: if a poor person gives sadaqah directly to a rich person or a Hashimi as a gift, it remains haram because ownership does not change. Also, if the mukatab is freed and becomes wealthy, leftover sadaqah is halal for him.
The key factor is whether ownership is transferred through a valid substitute (kitabah payment) or not.

تشریح قوله وما الى الخ- اگر مکاتب زکوۃ اور صدقہ و غیرہ کا مال لے کر بدل کتابت میں دے اور پھر باقی بدل کتابت کی ادائیگی سے عاجز ہو جائے تو آقا کے لئے وہ مال حلال ہے کیونکہ ملک بدل گئی یعنی مکاتب نے وہ مال بطور صدقہ لیا تھا اور آقا نے غلام آزاد کرنے کے عوض میں لیا ہے۔ اور شریعت میں ملک کا بدل جانا عین شی کے بدل جانے کے درجہ میں ہے حضرت بریرہ کے متعلق آنحضرت صلى الله عليه وسلم کے ارشاء هو لها صدقة ولنا هدية میں اس طرف اشارہ ہے۔

Book of Mukatab. 197. Ashraf al-Hidaya, Urdu commentary of Hidaya, volume 12. Ownership changes; similarly according to Imam Abu Yusuf, though in his view due to inability the master's ownership becomes settled, because the essence of sadaqah has no defect; the defect is in taking it because it contains humiliation. So it is not permissible for a wealthy person without necessity, nor for a Hashimi due to their extra respect. And taking it is not found from the master's side, so it becomes like a traveler who reached his homeland and a poor person became wealthy while they still have some sadaqah left, that remaining is halal for them. And upon this, if the mukatab is freed and becomes wealthy, whatever sadaqah remains with him is halal for him. Commentary on his saying 'and what to...' If the mukatab takes zakat and sadaqah and gives it as kitabah payment, then becomes unable from the remaining payment, that wealth is halal for the master because ownership changed: the mukatab took it as sadaqah, and the master took it as a substitute for freeing the slave. In shari'ah, change of ownership is like change of the thing itself; the Prophet's saying about Barira, 'it is sadaqah for her and a gift for us,' indicates this. Question: The ownership of the slave's body is the master's, so how can ownership change? Answer: The master's ownership of the body was dominated by the mukatab's ownership of possession, such that the mukatab could prevent the master from using his property, and the master had this ability. Then when the mukatab becomes unable, the situation reverses - it is not that ownership itself changes. His saying in the hadith of Barira - this hadith was authenticated by al-Shaykhayn (Bukhari and Muslim) from Aisha. The Prophet entered and saw a pot on the fire, then bread and some condiment were presented to him. He said: 'Don't I see the pot?' It was said: 'Meat given as sadaqah to Barira, and you do not eat sadaqah.' He said: 'It is sadaqah for her and a gift for us.' Explanation: The Prophet came home; a pot was on the stove; bread and the house's seasoning were brought before him. He said: 'Don't I see the pot?' It was answered: 'This is meat given as sadaqah to Barira, and you do not eat sadaqah.' The Prophet said: 'It is sadaqah for her and a gift for us.' His saying 'This is contrary to what...' - The mentioned ruling is contrary to when a poor person gives sadaqah to a rich person or a Hashimi as a gift. For example, saying 'Eat'; it will not be halal for them because the person for whom it is made permissible will take it under the ownership of the permitter, because the meaning of permitting is 'take whatever you wish from my property.' So ownership did not change, therefore the rich person and the Hashimi consuming it is not permissible. His saying 'If he becomes unable before payment...' - If the mentioned mukatab becomes unable before giving the sadaqah to the master, the ruling is the same: it is halal for the master. According to Imam Muhammad, because in his view ownership changes upon inability. According to Imam Abu Yusuf, ownership does not change upon inability but remains suspended; however, the sadaqah itself has no defect, the defect is in taking it (which involves humiliation), and since taking from the master's side did not occur, the master becomes like a traveler who took sadaqah due to need and then returned home, or a poor person who took sadaqah and then became wealthy and still has some sadaqah; the leftover sadaqah is halal in that state as well.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage from the Kitab al-Mukatab of Ashraf al-Hidayah discusses the ruling when a mukatab (a slave who has entered a contract of manumission) takes sadaqah (charity) and gives it to his master as payment for the kitabah contract, then subsequently becomes unable to pay the remaining amount. The master is allowed to keep that wealth as halal for himself, because the ownership of the sadaqah changed: the mukatab took it as sadaqah, but the master took it as payment for freeing the slave. The author cites the hadith of Barira (radiAllahu anha) where the Prophet (sallAllahu alayhi wa sallam) said regarding meat given as sadaqah to Barira that it is sadaqah for her but a gift for us. This indicates that the same item, when transferred from one person to another, changes its legal category. The passage also contrasts this with a case where a poor person gives sadaqah to a rich or Hashimi as a gift; since the ownership does not change, it remains prohibited for them. Finally, it mentions the view of Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad regarding whether ownership changes immediately upon inability or remains suspended; but the end result is the same that the leftover is halal.

Key Terms
مكاتَب (mukātab): A slave who has entered a contract (kitābah) with his master to pay a sum in exchange for his freedom.
كتابت (kitābah): The contract of manumission by which a slave agrees to pay his master a fixed amount for freedom.
صدقہ (ṣadaqah): Charity given voluntarily or as zakah; it is prohibited for the Prophet and his family (Hashimis) and for wealthy persons without need.
ملکیت (milkiyyah): Ownership; in this context, the change of ownership ('change of ownership is like change of the thing itself') is a legal principle that allows an item’s ruling to change.
عاجز ہونا (ʿājiz honā): To become unable to fulfill the kitabah payments.
ملکِ ید (milk al-yad): Possessory ownership; the right to use and benefit from an item, though the underlying legal ownership may belong to another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the sadaqah given by a mukatab to his master for kitabah considered halal for the master when the mukatab becomes unable?
A) Because the master is poor and allowed to take sadaqah.
B) Because ownership changes when the mukatab gives it as payment for kitabah.
C) Because the mukatab is free and can gift it to the master.
D) Because the master can take anything from his slave.

Key Takeaway
Sadaqah becomes halal for the master when the mukatab gives it as kitabah payment because change of ownership changes the legal status of the item.

Ruling on a Mukatab Who Commits a Crime Before Kitabah
Explaining options when a mukatab's crime is affected by subsequent inability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a slave commits a crime and later becomes a mukatab, and explain how the timing of judgment affects the master's options.

Core Idea
A mukatab's crime is temporarily prevented by kitabah; when kitabah ends, the original liability returns.

This lesson addresses a nuanced scenario in Hanafi fiqh involving slaves, crimes, and the kitabah contract. The source passage from Hidayah explains the options available to the master when a slave commits a crime and subsequently becomes a mukatab, and then becomes unable to complete the kitabah. We will trace the legal analysis, the role of the 'preventer' (mani'), and the intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, Abu Yusuf, and Zufar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
The passage covers: (1) crime before kitabah (master unaware), (2) crime during kitabah before judgment, (3) crime during kitabah after judgment. Recognise the differences.
2
Understand the principle of the temporary preventer
Kitabah acts as a temporary preventer (mani' qabil lil-zawal) blocking the master's right to hand over the slave. Once the mukatab becomes unable ('ajz), the preventer ends.
3
Analyse the effect of judgment
If judgment is passed during kitabah, the right transfers to the value (becomes a debt); otherwise, the original ruling (handover or ransom) returns upon 'ajz.
4
Compare with permanent preventers
Contrast with mudabbir and umm walad, where the preventer is permanent, so the slave is never handed over and value is always due.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf and tarjih
Zufar (and initially Abu Yusuf) held that even before judgment, the slave must be sold for value. The majority (Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, later Abu Yusuf) prevailed.

يسثير الى ان الواجب هو القيمة لا الاقل منها و من ارش الجناية، وهو مخالف لما ذكر من رواية الكرخي و المبسوط ان الواجب هو الاقل منهما وعلى هذا يكون تاويل كلامه اذا كانت القيمة اقل من ارش الجناية ٢ بنايي
Ashraf al-Hidayah, Urdu commentary of Hidayah, volume 12. 197 (page number repeated). Book of Mukatab. The slave committed a crime; his master made him a mukatab without knowing the crime; then the slave became unable; the master may hand over the slave or pay a ransom. He said: 'If the slave commits a crime, and his master makes him a mukatab without knowing the crime, then he becomes unable, he (the master) should hand him over or pay ransom, because this is the original ruling of the slave's crime. And he was not aware of the crime at the time of kitabah such that he would choose to pay ransom. However, the kitabah prevented handing over; when it ceases, the original ruling returns. Similarly, if the mukatab commits a crime and it is not adjudged before he becomes unable, due to what we said about the removal of the preventer. And if it is adjudged against him in his kitabah, then he becomes unable, it is a debt for which he will be sold, because the right transfers from the slave's body to its value by the judgment. This is the statement of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad; Abu Yusuf retracted to it (i.e., agreed). He initially used to say that he will be sold even if he becomes unable before judgment; that is Zufar's statement, because the preventer from handing over, i.e., kitabah, existed at the time of the crime, so as it occurred it necessitated the value, like in the crime of a mudabbir (slave promised freedom after master's death) and umm walad (slave mother of master's child). And our proof is that the preventer is subject to removal due to uncertainty, and transfer does not occur immediately, so it stays pending on judgment or consent. It is like a sold slave who runs away before delivery; the annulment of sale is pending on judgment due to uncertainty and possibility of return. This is unlike tadbir (promise of freedom) and istilad (motherhood), because these are not subject to removal in any case. Translation: The slave committed a crime and [the master] made him a mukatab without knowing the crime; then the slave became unable; so the master may hand over the slave or pay his ransom. Because the original ruling of the slave's crime is this, and the master did not know the crime at the time of kitabah such that he would choose ransom. But kitabah prevents handing over; when this preventer ends, the original ruling returns. Similarly, if the mukatab commits a crime and no judgment for ransom has occurred before he becomes unable, for the same reason of removal of preventer. And if judgment was passed against him during his kitabah, then he becomes unable, this is a debt for which he will be sold, because the right transfers from the body to value by judgment. This is the statement of the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), and Abu Yusuf retracted to it. He initially used to say that he will be sold even if he becomes unable before judgment; that is Zufar's statement, because the preventer, i.e., kitabah, existed at the time of crime. So when the crime occurred, it necessitated value, like in the crime of mudabbir and umm walad. Our proof is that the preventer is subject to removal due to uncertainty, and transfer does not occur immediately, so it stays pending on judgment or consent. It is like a sold slave who runs away before delivery; the annulment of sale is pending on judgment due to uncertainty and possibility of return. This is unlike tadbir and istilad because these are never subject to removal. Commentary... His saying: 'If a slave commits a crime...' The slave committed a crime; his master did not know about it; he made him a mukatab; then the mukatab became unable to pay the kitabah; the master has the option: either hand over the slave to the person whom he wronged, or pay compensation for his wrong. Because the original ruling of the slave's crime is this, and since the master was not aware of the crime at the time of kitabah, he was not choosing ransom... [Footnote: It is sufficient that the wajib is the value, not the lesser of the two, and the arsh (diya) of the crime, which contradicts what was mentioned from the narration of al-Karkhi and al-Mabsut that the wajib is the lesser of the two. Therefore, his interpretation is when the value is less than the arsh of the crime.]
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage addresses a scenario where a slave commits a crime, and then his master makes him a mukatab (enters into a kitabah contract) without knowing about the crime. Later the mukatab becomes unable to pay the kitabah. In this situation, the master has a choice: he can hand over the slave to the victim to satisfy the crime, or he can pay the ransom (compensation) on behalf of the slave. The original ruling for a slave's crime is that the master either hands over the slave or pays the ransom; but the kitabah contract acts as a preventer (mani') from handing over the slave (because the mukatab has rights). When the mukatab becomes unable, the kitabah dissolves and the preventer is removed, so the original ruling returns. The text then discusses a second scenario: if the mukatab commits a crime during his kitabah period, and before a judgment is given regarding compensation he becomes unable to pay the kitabah, the same ruling applies (the preventer ends, original ruling returns). However, if judgment already occurred during the kitabah (ordering payment of value), then that value becomes a debt; the slave will be sold to pay it. The text records an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold this view; Abu Yusuf initially agreed with Zufar that even before judgment, if 'ajz occurs, the slave is sold, but later retracted to the majority view. The proof for the majority is that kitabah is a temporary preventer due to its nature of uncertainty; the right does not transfer immediately but is suspended until judgment or consent. This is analogous to a sold slave who runs away: the sale is not automatically annulled but is pending judgment. This is unlike tadbir (promise of freedom after death) and istilad (mother of the child) which are permanent preventers that never cease.

Key Terms
جنایت (jināyah): Criminal offense (homicide, injury, etc.) committed by a slave, for which compensation is due.
مكاتبت (mukātibah): The state of being a mukatab (slave under kitabah contract).
مدبر (mudabbir): A slave whom the master has promised freedom after his (master's) death by saying 'you are free after my death.'
ام ولد (umm walad): A slave-woman who bears a child for her master; she becomes free upon the master's death and cannot be sold.
مانع (māniʿ): Preventer, an obstruction that blocks a legal ruling from applying.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The master may hand over the slave.
B) The slave must be sold to pay the value.
C) The crime is waived due to kitabah.
D) The master must pay the ransom from his own wealth.

Key Takeaway
A kitabah contract temporarily prevents the master from handing over the slave for a crime, but the original liability returns when the preventer ends, unless judgment transfers the right to a debt.

Rulings on Contracts Made Under Coercion
States the ruling on sale, purchase, iqrar, and lease when done under coercion, with option to annul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on contracts made under coercion and apply the distinction between severe and minor coercion to determine whether the contract is binding or subject to the option of annulment.

Core Idea
Coercion negates consent, so the contract is valid but the coerced party has the option to annul.

This lesson addresses a common yet nuanced area of Hanafi fiqh: what happens when a person is forced to sell, buy, lease, or make a legal acknowledgment under threat? The passage from Al-Hidayah presents a clear ruling with a Quranic proof, distinguishing between severe coercion (which gives an option) and minor coercion (which does not). Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the law in real transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contracts and the ruling
List the contracts mentioned: sale, purchase, lease, and legal acknowledgment (iqrar). The ruling: the coerced party has the option (khiyar) to confirm or annul the contract.
The option applies only to the coerced party, not the oppressor.
2
Locate the dalil
The Quranic verse 4:29 requiring mutual consent ('an taradin) is cited. Coercion negates consent, so the contract is defective unless the option is exercised.
The dalil is qati' in thubut and dalalah.
3
Distinguish severe vs minor coercion
Severe coercion (death, severe beating, imprisonment) gives the option. Minor coercion (light whip, short imprisonment) does not, unless the person is of high status who would be humiliated.
The criterion is whether a reasonable person would fear the threat enough to lose consent.
4
Note the special case of iqrar
Acknowledgment (iqrar) is a proof because truthfulness is presumed. Even under coercion, it may be valid, but the text suggests it is not automatically accepted
The passage does not fully elaborate the ruling on iqrar; it hints at a different treatment.
5
Examine cross-madhhab dispute
Other schools may rule a coerced contract void automatically. The Hanafi school rules it valid with option, preserving the coerced party's capacity while protecting consent.
The thamarah al-khilaf is that under other schools, no option exists; the contract is void from inception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال و اذا اكره الرجل على بيع ماله او على شراء سلعة أو على ان يقر لرجل بالف او يواجر داره واکره علی لك بالقتل او بالضرب الشديد أو بالحبس فباع او اشترى فهو بالخيار ان شاء أمضى البيع وان شاء فسخه ورجع بالمبيع لان من شرط صحة هذه العقود التراضى قال الله تعالى الا ان تكون تجارة عن راض منكم والاكراه بهذه الاشياء يُعدم الرضاء فتفسد بخلاف ما اذا اكره بضرب سوط او حبس بعد قيد يوم لانه لا يبالي به بالنظر الى العادة فلا يتحقق به الاكراه الا اذا كان الرجل صاحب منصب يعلم انه يستضر به لقوات الرضاء وكذا الاقرار حجة لترجح جنبة الصدق فيه على جنبة الكذب وعند الاكراه
He said: When a man is coerced to sell his property, or to buy a commodity, or to acknowledge a debt of one thousand, or to lease his house, and he is coerced to do that by threat of death, severe beating, or imprisonment, and he sells or buys, then he has a choice: if he wishes he may confirm the sale, and if he wishes he may annul it and take back the sold item. Because among the conditions for the validity of these contracts is mutual consent. Allah Almighty says: 'except it be a trade by mutual consent from you' (4:29). Coercion by these things negates consent, so it becomes void (defective). Contrast with when he is coerced by a beating with a whip or imprisonment for one day or confinement, because he does not care about that according to custom, so coercion is not realized thereby, unless the man is of high status and knows that he will be harmed by it due to loss of consent. And likewise, acknowledgment (iqrar) is a proof because the side of truthfulness outweighs falsehood, and under coercion...
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage addresses the legal effect of coercion on contracts of exchange (sale, purchase, lease) and on legal acknowledgments (iqrar). The ruling is: if a person is forced under threat of death, severe beating, or imprisonment to sell his property or make any such contract, the contract is not void ab initio but rather the coerced party has an option (khiyar) to either confirm or rescind the transaction. The proof is the Quranic verse requiring mutual consent (4:29). Coercion negates consent, so the contract is defective but not entirely void because the coerced person still has legal capacity. However, if the coercion is minor, such as a light whip or a short imprisonment, it does not constitute legal coercion because people ordinarily do not fear such punishment enough to lose their consent. An exception is made for persons of high rank who may be humiliated even by light punishment. For acknowledgments (iqrar), the ruling is different: iqrar is a legal proof because the side of truthfulness is stronger than falsehood. Even under coercion, an iqrar may be considered valid if there is some indication of truth, but the text suggests that under coercion, it is not automatically accepted.

Key Terms
الخيار (al-khiyar): The option given to the coerced party to either confirm or annul the contract.
الرضا (al-rida): Mutual consent, a condition for validity of contracts.
الإقرار (al-iqrar): Legal acknowledgment, considered a proof due to the prevalence of truthfulness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is considered severe coercion that gives the option to annul?
A) A threat of a light slap
B) Threat of death
C) A fine of 100 dirhams
D) Disapproval from family

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a contract made under severe coercion is valid but the coerced party has the option to confirm or annul it, based on the Quranic requirement of mutual consent.

Coerced Contracts: Ruling, Proof, and the Option to Rescind
Ruling with proof plus expansion into general principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on coerced sales and purchases, explain the Quranic proof requiring mutual consent, and distinguish between rescindable and non-rescindable coerced actions, including identifying the inter-madhhab difference.

Core Idea
A coerced contract is valid (mun'aqid) but rescindable; the coerced party may affirm or rescind after coercion ends.

This passage from Al-Hidayah tackles the delicate balance between contract stability and justice. When a person is forced to sell or buy under threat of death, severe beating, or imprisonment, the contract is not automatically void. Instead, the Hanafi school treats it as validly concluded (mun'aqid) but grants the coerced party an option to rescind (khiyar al-faskh) once the coercion lifts. The Quranic verse on mutual consent (4:29) is the primary evidence, and the passage expands into a general principle covering all coerced verbal acts, including a significant difference with the other three Sunni schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the coerced acts and the ruling
The passage lists sale, purchase, rental, and acknowledgment of a debt under severe coercion (death, severe beating, imprisonment). The ruling: the coerced party has khiyar (option) to affirm or rescind the contract.
Note the Arabic: 'فهو بالخيار' – he has the option.
2
Locate the dalil and analyze its wajh al-dalalah
The proof is Quran 4:29: 'except that it be trade by mutual consent.' The phrase 'عن تراض' indicates that mutual consent is a condition for valid trade. Coercion eliminates consent, making the contract fâsid (rescindable).
The commentary addresses objections about the verse 'أحل الله البيع' – the contract is fâsid, not bâtil, because the core exchange exists.
3
Distinguish severe vs. minor coercion
Minor threats (one whip, one day imprisonment) do not constitute legal coercion because customarily they are disregarded. Exception: a man of high rank who would suffer loss of respect even from minor coercion.
The criterion is 'لا يبالي به بالنظر إلى العادة' – customarily not cared about.
4
Apply the general Hanafi principle to non-rescindable actions
All coerced verbal actions are mun'aqid. Those susceptible to rescission (sale, lease) can be rescinded. Those not susceptible (talaq, nikah, ʿitq, tadbir, istila, nadhr) become binding (lazim) immediately.
This is the 'qa'ida kulliyya' mentioned in the commentary.
5
Identify the inter-madhhab dispute
The three other schools (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) hold that coerced actions like talaq are not binding at all. The Hanafi response distinguishes between the existence of the contract (inqidâ') and its binding nature (luzûm).
Thamarah al-khilaf: In Hanafi law, a coerced divorce is effective; in other schools, it is not.
